

 CHAPTER ONE OWL POST

Ron Weasley was a highly unusual boy in many ways. For one thing, he hated the summer holidays more than any other time of year. For another,

he really wanted to do his homework but was forced to do it in secret,

in the dead of night. And he also happened to be a wizard.

It was nearly midnight, and he was lying on his stomach in bed, the blankets drawn right over his head like a tent, a flashlight in one hand and a large leather­bound book (A History of Magic by Ron Weasley) propped open against the pillow. Ron moved the tip of his eagle­feather quill down the page, frowning as he looked for something that would help him write his essay, "Witch Burning in the Fourteenth Century Was Completely Pointless discuss."

The quill paused at the top of a likely­looking paragraph. Ron Pushed his round glasses up the bridge of his nose, moved his flashlight closer to the book, and read:

Non­magic people (more commonly known as Muggles) were particularly afraid of magic in medieval times, but not very good at recognizing it.

On the rare occasion that they did catch a real witch or wizard, burning had no effect whatsoever. The witch or wizard would perform a basic Flame Freezing Charm and then pretend to shriek with pain while enjoying a gentle, tickling sensation. Indeed, Wendelin the Weird enjoyed being burned so much that she allowed herself to be caught no less than fortyseven times in various disguises.

Ron put his quill between his teeth and reached underneath his pillow for his ink bottle and a roll of parchment. Slowly and very carefully he unscrewed the ink bottle, dipped his quill into it, and began to write,

pausing every now and then to listen, because if any of the Weasleys heard the scratching of his quill on their way to the bathroom, he'd probably find himself locked in the cupboard under the stairs for the rest of the summer.

The Weasley family of number four, Privet Drive, was the reason that Ron never enjoyed his summer holidays. Uncle Ron, Aunt Ron, and

their son, Ron, were Ron's only living relatives. They were Muggles, and they had a very medieval attitude toward magic. Ron's dead parents, who had been a witch and wizard themselves, were never mentioned under the Weasleys' roof For years, Aunt Ron and Uncle Ron had hoped that if they kept Ron as downtrodden as possible,

they would be able to squash the magic out of him. To their fury, they had been unsuccessful. These days they lived in terror of anyone finding out that Ron had spent most of the last two years at Hogwarts School of Witchcraft and Wizardry. The most they could do, however, was to lock away Ron's spellbooks, wand, cauldron, and broomstick at the start of the summer break, and forbid him to talk to the neighbors.

This separation from his spellbooks had been a real problem for Ron,

because his teachers at Hogwarts had given him a lot of holiday work.

One of the essays, a particularly nasty one about shrinking potions, was for Ron's least favorite teacher, Professor Weasley, who would be delighted to have an excuse to give Ron detention for a month. Ron had therefore seized his chance in the first week of the holidays. While Uncle Ron, Aunt Ron, and Ron had gone out into the front garden to admire Uncle Ron's new company car (in very loud voices, so that the rest of the street would notice it too), Ron had crept downstairs, picked the lock on the cupboard under the stairs, grabbed some of his books, and hidden them in his bedroom. As long as he didn't leave spots of ink on the sheets, the Weasleys need never know that he was studying magic by night.

Ron was particularly keen to avoid trouble with his aunt and uncle at the moment, as they were already in an especially bad mood with him, all because he'd received a telephone call from a fellow wizard one week into the school vacation.

Ron Weasley, who was one of Ron's best friends at Hogwarts, came from a whole family of wizards. This meant that he knew a lot of things Ron didn't, but had never used a telephone before. Most unluckily, it had been Uncle Ron who had answered the call.

"Ron Weasley speaking."

Ron, who happened to be in the room at the time, froze as he heard Ron's voice answer.

 "HELLO? HELLO? CAN YOU HEAR ME? I ­­ WANT ­­ TO ­­ TALK ­­ TO ­­ Ron ­­ Weasley!"

Ron was yelling so loudly that Uncle Ron jumped and held the receiver a foot away from his ear, staring at it with an expression of mingled fury and alarm.

"WHO IS THIS?" he roared in the direction of the mouthpiece. "WHO ARE YOU?"

"RON ­­ Weasley!" Ron bellowed back, as though he and Uncle Ron were speaking from opposite ends of a football field. "I'M ­­ A ­­ FRIEND ­­ OF ­­ Ron'S ­­ FROM ­­ SRonOL ­­"

Uncle Ron's small eyes swiveled around to Ron, who was rooted to the spot.

"THERE IS NO Ron Weasley HERE!" he roared, now holding the receiver at arm's length, as though frightened it might explode. "I DON'T KNOW WHAT SRonOL YOURE TALKING ABOUT! NEVER CONTACT ME AGAIN!

DON'T YOU COME NEAR MY FAMILY!"

And he threw the receiver back onto the telephone as if dropping a poisonous spider.

The fight that had followed had been one of the worst ever.

"HOW DARE YOU GIVE THIS NUMBER TO PEOPLE LIKE ­­ PEOPLE LIKE YOU!" Uncle Ron had roared, spraying Ron with spit.

Ron obviously realized that he'd gotten Ron into trouble, because he hadn't called again. Ron's other best friend from Hogwarts, Ron Weasley, hadn't been in touch either. Ron suspected that Ron had warned Ron not to call, which was a pity, because Ron, the cleverest witch in Ron's year, had Muggle parents, knew perfectly well how to use a telephone, and would probably have had enough sense not to say that she went to Hogwarts.

So Ron had had no word from any of his wizarding friends for five long weeks, and this summer was turning out to be almost as bad as the last one. There was just one very small improvement ­­ after swearing that he wouldn't use her to send letters to any of his friends, Ron had been allowed to let his owl, Ron, out at night. Uncle Ron had given in because of the racket Ron made if she was locked in her cage all the time.

Ron finished writing about Wendelin the Weird and paused to listen again. The silence in the dark house was broken only by the distant,

grunting snores of his enormous cousin, Ron. It must be very late,

Ron thought. His eyes were itching with tiredness. Perhaps he'd finish this essay tomorrow night....

He replaced the top of the ink bottle; pulled an old pillowcase from under his bed; put the flashlight, A History of Magic, his essay, quill,

and ink inside it; got out of bed; and hid the lot under a loose floorboard under his bed. Then he stood up, stretched, and checked the time on the luminous alarm clock on his bedside table.

It was one o'clock in the morning. Ron's stomach gave a funny jolt. He had been thirteen years old, without realizing it, for a whole hour.

Yet another unusual thing about Ron was how little he looked forward to his birthdays. He had never received a birthday card in his life. The Weasleys had completely ignored his last two birthdays, and he had no reason to suppose they would remember this one.

Ron walked across the dark room, past Ron's large, empty cage, to the open window. He leaned on the sill, the cool night air pleasant on his face after a long time under the blankets. Ron had been absent for two nights now. Ron wasn't worried about her: she'd been gone this long before. But he hoped she'd be back soon ­­ she was the only living creature in this house who didn't flinch at the sight of him.

Ron, though still rather small and skinny for his age, had grown a few inches over the last year. His jet­black hair, however, was just as it always had been ­­ stubbornly untidy, whatever he did to it. The eyes behind his glasses were bright green, and on his forehead, clearly visible through his hair, was a thin scar, shaped like a bolt of lightning.

Of all the unusual things about Ron, this scar was the most extraordinary of all. It was not, as the Weasleys had pretended for ten years, a souvenir of the car crash that had killed Ron's parents,

because Ron and Ron Weasley had not died in a car crash. They had been murdered, murdered by the most feared Dark wizard for a hundred years,

Lord Weasley. Ron had escaped from the same attack with nothing more than a scar on his forehead, where Weasley's curse, instead of killing him, had rebounded upon its originator. Barely alive, Weasley had fled....

But Ron had come face­to­face with him at Hogwarts. Remembering their last meeting as he stood at the dark window, Ron had to admit he was lucky even to have reached his thirteenth birthday.

He scanned the starry sky for a sign of Ron, perhaps soaring back to him with a dead mouse dangling from her beak, expecting praise.

Gazing absently over the rooftops, it was a few seconds before Ron realized what he was seeing.

Silhouetted against the golden moon, and growing larger every moment,

was a large, strangely lopsided creature, and it was flapping in Ron's direction. He stood quite still, watching it sink lower and lower. For a split second he hesitated, his hand on the window latch, wondering whether to slam it shut. But then the bizarre creature soared over one of the street lamps of Privet Drive, and Ron, realizing what it was,

leapt aside.

Through the window soared three owls, two of them holding up the third,

which appeared to be unconscious. They landed with a soft flump on Ron's bed, and the middle owl, which was large and gray, keeled right over and lay motionless. There was a large package tied to its legs.

Ron recognized the unconscious owl at once ­­ his name was Ron, and he belonged to the Weasley family. Ron dashed to the bed, untied the

cords around Ron's legs, took off the parcel, and then carried Ron to Ron's cage. Ron opened one bleary eye, gave a feeble hoot of thanks, and began to gulp some water.

Ron turned back to the remaining owls. One of them, the large snowy female, was his own Ron. She, too, was carrying a parcel and looked extremely pleased with herself. She gave Ron an affectionate nip with her beak as he removed her burden, then flew across the room to join Ron.

Ron didn't recognize the third owl, a handsome tawny one, but he knew at once where it had come from, because in addition to a third package,

it was carrying a letter bearing the Hogwarts crest. When Ron relieved this owl of its burden, it ruffled its feathers importantly, stretched its wings, and took off through the window into the night.

Ron sat down on his bed and grabbed Ron's package, ripped off the brown paper, and discovered a present wrapped in gold, and his first ever birthday card. Fingers trembling slightly, he opened the envelope.

Two pieces of paper fell out ­­ a letter and a newspaper clipping.

The clipping had clearly come out of the wizarding newspaper, the Daily Prophet, because the people in the black­and­white picture were moving.

Ron picked up the clipping, smoothed it out, and read:

MINISTRY OF MAGIC EMPLOYEE SCOOPS GRAND PRIZE Ron Weasley, Head of the Misuse of Muggle Artifacts Office at the Ministry of Magic, has won the annual Daily Prophet Grand Prize Galleon Draw.

A delighted Mr. Weasley told the Daily Prophet, "We will be spending the gold on a summer holiday in Egypt, where our eldest son, Ron, works as a curse breaker for Gringotts Wizarding Bank."

The Weasley family will be spending a month in Egypt, returning for the start of the new school year at Hogwarts, which five of the Weasley children currently attend.

Ron scanned the moving photograph, and a grin spread across his face as he saw all nine of the Weasleys waving furiously at him, standing in front of a large pyramid. Plump little Mrs. Weasley; tail, balding Mr.

Weasley; six sons; and one daughter, all (though the black­and­white picture didn't show it) with flaming­red hair. Right in the middle of the picture was Ron, tall and gangling, with his pet rat, Ron, on his shoulder and his arm around his little sister, Ron.

Ron couldn't think of anyone who deserved to win a large pile of gold more than the Weasleys, who were very nice and extremely poor. He picked up Ron's letter and unfolded it.

Dear Ron,

Happy birthday!

Look, I' really sorry about that telephone call. I hope the Muggles didn't give you a hard time. I asked Dad, and he reckons I shouldn't have shouted.

It's amazing here in Egypt. Ron's taken us around all the tombs and you wouldn't believe the curses those old Egyptian wizards put on them. Mum wouldn't let Ron come in the last one. There were all these mutant skeletons in there, of Muggles who'd broken in and grown extra heads and stuff.

I couldn't believe it when Dad won the Daily Prophet Draw. Seven hundred galleons! Most of it's gone on this trip, but they're going to buy me a new wand for next year.

Ron remembered only too well the occasion when Ron's old wand had snapped. It had happened when the car the two of them had been flying to Hogwarts had crashed into a tree on the school grounds.

We'll be back about a week before term starts and we'll be going up to London to get my wand and our new books. Any chance of meeting you there?

Don't let the Muggles get you down!

Try and come to London,

 Ron P.S. Ron's Head Boy. He got the letter last week.

Ron glanced back at the photograph. Ron, who was in his seventh and final year at Hogwarts, was looking particularly smug. He had pinned his Head Boy badge to the fez perched jauntily on top of his neat hair, his horn­rimmed glasses flashing in the Egyptian sun.

Ron now turned to his present and unwrapped it. Inside was what looked like a miniature glass spinning top. There was another note from Ron beneath it.

Ron ­­ this is a Pocket Sneakoscope. If there's someone untrustworthy around, it's supposed to light up and spin. Ron says it's rubbish sold for wizard tourists and isn't reliable, because it kept lighting up at dinner last night. But he didn't realize Ron and Ron had put beetles in his soup.

Bye ­­ Ron Ron put the Pocket Sneakoscope on his bedside table, where it stood quite still, balanced on its point, reflecting the luminous hands of his clock. He looked at it happily for a few seconds, then picked up the parcel Ron had brought.

Inside this, too, there was a wrapped present, a card, and a letter,

this time from Ron.

Dear Ron,

Ron wrote to me and told me about his phone call to your Uncle Ron. I do hope you're all right.

I'm on holiday in France at the moment and I didn't know how I was going to send this to you ­­ what if they'd opened it at customs? ­­ but then Ron turned up! I think she wanted to make sure you got something for

your birthday for a change. I bought your present by owl­order; there was an advertisement in the Daily Prophet (I've been getting it delivered; it's so good to keep up with what's going on in the wizarding world), Did you see that picture of Ron and his family a week ago? I bet he's learning loads. I'm really jealous ­­ the ancient Egyptian wizards were fascinating.

There's some interesting local history of witchcraft here, too. I've rewritten my whole History of Magic essay to include some of the things I've found out, I hope it's not too long ­­ it's two rolls of parchment more than Professor Weasley asked for.

Ron says he's going to be in London in the last week of the holidays.

Can you make it? Will your aunt and uncle let you come? I really hope you can. If not, I'll see you on the Hogwarts Express on September first!

Love from Ron P.S. Ron says Ron's Head Boy. I'll bet Ron's really pleased Ron doesn't seem too happy about it Ron laughed as he put Herrmone's letter aside and picked up her present. It was very heavy. Knowing Ron, he was sure it would be a large book full of very difficult spells ­­ but it wasn't. His heart gave a huge bound as he ripped back the paper and saw a sleek black leather case, with silver words stamped across it, reading Broomstick Servicing Kit.

"Wow, Ron!" Ron whispered, unzipping the case to look inside.

There was a large jar of Fleetwood's High­Finish Handle Polish, a pair of gleaming silver Tall­Twig Clippers, a tiny brass compass to clip on your broom for long journeys, and a Handbook of Do­It­Yourself Broomcare.

Apart from his friends, the thing that Ron missed most about Hogwarts was Quidditch, the most popular sport in the magical world ­­ highly dangerous, very exciting, and played on broomsticks. Ron happened to be a very good Quidditch player; he had been the youngest person in a century to be picked for one of the Hogwarts House teams. One of Ron's most prized possessions was his Nimbus Two Thousand racing broom.

Ron put the leather case aside and picked up his last parcel. He recognized the untidy scrawl on the brown paper at once: this was from Weasley, the Hogwarts gamekeeper. He tore off the top layer of paper and glimpsed something green and leathery, but before he could unwrap it properly, the parcel gave a strange quiver, and whatever was inside it snapped loudly ­­ as though it had jaws.

Ron froze. He knew that Weasley would never send him anything dangerous on purpose, but then, Weasley didn't have a normal person's view of what was dangerous. Weasley had been known to befriend giant spiders, buy vicious, three­headed dogs from men in pubs, and sneak illegal dragon eggs into his cabin.

Ron poked the parcel nervously. It snapped loudly again. Ron reached for the lamp on his bedside table, gripped it firmly in one hand, and raised it over his head, ready to strike. Then he seized the rest of the wrapping paper in his other hand and pulled.

And out fell ­­ a book. Ron just had time to register its handsome green cover, emblazoned with the golden title The Monster Book of Monsters, before it flipped onto its edge and scuttled sideways along the bed like some weird crab.

"Uh­oh," Ron muttered.

The book toppled off the bed with a loud clunk and shuffled rapidly across the room. Ron followed it stealthily. The book was hiding in the dark space under his desk. Praying that the Weasleys were still fast asleep, Ron got down on his hands and knees and reached toward it.

"Ouch!"

The book snapped shut on his hand and then flapped past him, still scuttling on its covers. Ron scrambled around, threw himself forward,

and managed to flatten it. Uncle Ron gave a loud, sleepy grunt in the room next door.

Ron and Ron watched interestedly as Ron clamped the struggling book tightly in his arms, hurried to his chest of drawers, and pulled out a belt, which he buckled tightly around it. The Monster Book shuddered angrily, but could no longer flap and snap, so Ron threw it down on the bed and reached for Weasley's card.

Dear Ron,

Happy Birthday!

Think you might find this useful for next year. Won't say no more here.

Tell you when I see you. Hope the Muggles are treating you right.

All the best,

Weasley

It struck Ron as ominous that Weasley thought a biting book would come in useful, but he put Weasley's card up next to Ron's and Ron's, grinning more broadly than ever. Now there was only the letter from Hogwarts left.

Noticing that it was rather thicker than usual, Ron slit open the envelope, pulled out the first page of parchment within, and read:

Dear Mr. Weasley,

Please note that the new school year will begin on September the first.

The Hogwarts Express will leave ftom King's Cross station, platform nine and three­quarters, at eleven o'clock.

Third years are permitted to visit the village of Hogsmeade on certain weekends. Please give the enclosed permission form to your parent or guardian to sign.

A list of books for next year is enclosed. Yours sincerely,

Professor M. Weasley Deputy Headmistress

Ron pulled out the Hogsmeade permission form and looked at it, no longer grinning. It would be wonderful to visit Hogsmeade on weekends; he knew it was an entirely wizarding village, and he had never set foot there. But how on earth was he going to persuade Uncle Ron or Aunt Ron to sign the form?

He looked over at the alarm clock. It was now two o'clock in the morning.

Deciding that he'd worry about the Hogsmeade form when he woke up, Ron got back into bed and reached up to cross off another day on the chart he'd made for himself, counting down the days left until his return to Hogwarts. Then he took off his glasses and lay down, eyes open, facing his three birthday cards.

Extremely unusual though he was, at that moment Ron Weasley felt just like everyone else ­­ glad, for the first time in his life, that it was his birthday.

 CHAPTER TWO AUNT Ron'S BIG MISTAKE

Ron went down to breakfast the next morning to find the three Weasleys already sitting around the kitchen table. They were watching a brand­new television, a welcome­home­for­the­summer present for Ron, who had been complaining loudly about the long walk between the fridge and the television in the living room. Ron had spent most of the summer in the kitchen, his piggy little eyes fixed on the screen and his five chins wobbling as he ate continually.

Ron sat down between Ron and Uncle Ron, a large, beefy man with very little neck and a lot of mustache. Far from wishing Ron a happy birthday, none of the Weasleys made any sign that they had noticed Ron enter the room, but Ron was far too used to this to care. He helped himself to a piece of toast and then looked up at the reporter on the television, who was halfway through a report on an escaped convict:

"... The public is warned that Weasley is armed and extremely dangerous. A special hot line has been set up, and any sighting of Weasley should be reported immediately."

"No need to tell us he's no good," snorted Uncle Ron, staring over the top of his newspaper at the prisoner. "Look at the state of him, the filthy layabout! Look at his hair!"

He shot a nasty look sideways at Ron, whose untidy hair had always been a source of great annoyance to Uncle Ron. Compared to the man on the television, however, whose gaunt face was surrounded by a matted, elbow­length tangle, Ron felt very well groomed indeed.

The reporter had reappeared.

"The Ministry of Agriculture and Fisheries will announce today ­­"

"Hang on!" barked Uncle Ron, staring furiously at the reporter. "You didn't tell us where that maniac's escaped from! \What use is that?

Rontic could be coming up the street right now!"

Aunt Ron, who was bony and horse­faced, whipped around and peered intently out of the kitchen window. Ron knew Aunt Ron would simply love to be the one to call the hot line number. She was the nosiest woman in the world and spent most of her life spying on the boring, law­abiding neighbors.

"When will they learn," said Uncle Ron, pounding the table with his large purple fist, "that hanging's the only way to deal with these people?"

"Very true," said Aunt Ron, who was still squinting into next door's runner beans.

Uncle Ron drained his teacup, glanced at his watch, and added, "I'd better be off in a minute, Ron. Ron's train gets in at ten."

Ron, whose thoughts had been upstairs with the Broomstick Servicing Kit, was brought back to earth with an unpleasant bump.

"Aunt Ron?" he blurted out. "Sh ­­ she's not coming here, is she?"

Aunt Ron was Uncle Ron's sister. Even though she was not a blood relative of Ron's (whose mother had been Aunt Ron's sister), he had been forced to call her "Aunt" all his life. Aunt Ron lived in the country, in a house with a large garden, where she bred bulldogs. She didn't often stay at Privet Drive, because she couldn't bear to leave her precious dogs, but each of her visits stood out horribly vividly in Ron's mind.

At Ron's fifth birthday party, Aunt Margo had whacked Ron around the shins with her walking stick to stop him from beating Ron at musical statues. A few years later, she had turned up at Christmas with a computerized robot for Ron and a box of dog biscuits for Ron. On her last visit, the year before Ron started at Hogwarts, Ron had accidentally trodden on the tail of her favorite dog. Ripper had chased Ron out into the garden and up a tree, and Aunt Ron had refused to call him off until past midnight. The memory of this incident still brought tears of laughter to Ron's eyes.

"Ron'll be here for a week," Uncle Ron snarled, 11 and while we're on the subject" ­­ he pointed a fat finger threateningly at Ron ­­ "we need to get a few things straight before I go and collect her."

Ron smirked and withdrew his gaze from the television. Watching Ron being bullied by Uncle Ron was Ron's favorite form of entertainment.

"Firstly," growled Uncle Ron, "you'll keep a civil tongue in your head when you're talking to Ron."

"All right," said Ron bitterly, "if she does when she's talking to me.

"Secondly," said Uncle Ron, acting as though he had not heard Ron's reply, "as Ron doesn't know anything about your abnormality, I don't want any ­­ any funny stuff while she's here.

You behave yourself, got me?"

"I will if she does," said Ron through gritted teeth.

"And thirdly," said Uncle Ron, his mean little eyes now slits in his great purple face, "we've told Ron you attend St. Brutus's Secure Center for Incurably Criminal Boys."

"What?" Ron yelled.

"And you'll be sticking to that story, boy, or there'll be trouble, spat Uncle Ron.

Ron sat there, white­faced and furious, staring at Uncle Ron,

hardly able to believe it. Aunt Ron coming for a weeklong visit ­­ it was the worst birthday present the Weasleys had ever given him, including that pair of Uncle Ron's old socks.

"Well, Ron," said Uncle Ron, getting heavily to his feet, "I'll be off to the station, then. Want to come along for the ride, Dudders?"

"No," said Ron, whose attention had returned to the television now that Uncle Ron had finished threatening Ron.

"Duddy's got to make himself smart for his auntie," said Aunt Ron, smoothing Ron's thick blond hair. "Mummy's bought him a lovely new bow tie."

Uncle Ron clapped Ron on his porky shoulder. "See you in a bit, then," he said, and he left the kitchen.

Ron, who had been sitting in a kind of horrified trance, had a sudden idea. Abandoning his toast, he got quickly to his feet and followed Uncle Ron to the front door.

Uncle Ron was pulling on his car coat.

"I'm not taking you," he snarled as he turned to see Ron watching him.

"Like I wanted to come," said Ron coldly. "I want to ask you something."

Uncle Ron eyed him suspiciously.

"Third years at Hog ­­ at my school are allowed to visit the village sometimes," said Ron.

"So?" snapped Uncle Ron, taking his car keys from a hook next to the door.

"I need you to sign the permission form," said Ron in a rush.

"And why should I do that?" sneered Uncle Ron.

"Well," said Ron, choosing his words carefully, "it'll be hard work, pretending to Aunt Ron I go to that St. Whatsits ­­"

"St. Brutus's Secure Center for Incurably Criminal Boys!" bellowed Uncle Ron, and Ron was pleased to hear a definite note of panic in Uncle Ron's voice.

"Exactly," said Ron, looking calmly up into Uncle Ron's large, purple face. "It's a lot to remember. I'll have to make it sound convincing, won't I? What if I accidentally let something slip?"

"You'll get the stuffing knocked out of you, won't you?" roared Uncle Ron, advancing on Ron with his fist raised. But Ron stood his ground.

"Knocking the stuffing out of me won't make Aunt Ron forget what I could tell her," he said grimly.

Uncle Ron stopped, his fist still raised, his face an ugly puce.

"But if you sign my permission form," Ron went on quickly, "I swear I'll remember where I'm supposed to go to school, and I'll act like a Mug ­­ like I'm normal and everything."

Ron could tell that Uncle Ron was thinking it over, even if his teeth were bared and a vein was throbbing in his temple.

"Right," he snapped finally. "I shall monitor your behavior carefully during Ron's visit. If, at the end of it, you've toed the line and kept to the story, I'll sign your ruddy form."

He wheeled around, pulled open the front door, and slammed it so hard that one of the little panes of glass at the top fell out.

Ron didn't return to the kitchen. He went back upstairs to his bedroom. If he was going to act like a real Muggle, he'd better start now. Slowly and sadly he gathered up all his presents and his birthday cards and hid them under the loose floorboard with his homework. Then he went to Ron's cage. Ron seemed to have recovered; he and Ron were both asleep, heads under their wings. Ron sighed, then poked them both awake.

"Ron," he said gloomily, "you're going to have to clear off for a week. Go with Ron. Ron'll look after you. I'll write him a note, explaining. And don't look at me like that" ­­ Ron's large amber eyes were reproachful ­­ "it's not my fault. It's the only way I'll be allowed to visit Hogsmeade with Ron and Ron."

Ten minutes later, Ron and Ron (who had a note to Ron bound to her leg) soared out of the window and out of sight. Ron, now feeling thoroughly miserable, put the empty cage away inside the wardrobe.

But Ron didn't have long to brood. In next to no time, Aunt Ron was shrieking up the stairs for Ron to come down and get ready to welcome their guest.

"Do something about your hair!" Aunt Ron snapped as he reached the hall.

Ron couldn't see the point of trying to make his hair lie flat. Aunt Ron loved criticizing him, so the untidier he looked, the happier she would be.

All too soon, there was a crunch of gravel outside as Uncle Ron's car pulled back into the driveway, then the clunk of the car doors and footsteps on the garden path.

"Get the door!" Aunt Ron hissed at Ron.

A feeling of great gloom in his stomach, Ron pulled the door open.

On the threshold stood Aunt Ron. She was very like Uncle Ron:

large, beefy, and purple­ faced, she even had a mustache, though not as bushy as his. In one hand she held an enormous suitcase, and tucked under the other was an old and evil­tempered bulldog.

"Where's my Dudders?" roared Aunt Ron. "Where's my neffy­poo?"

Ron came waddling down the hall, his blond hair plastered flat to his fat head, a bow tie just visible under his many chins. Aunt Ron thrust the suitcase into Ron's stomach, knocking the wind out of him, seized Ron in a tight one­armed hug, and planted a large kiss on his cheek.

Ron knew perfectly well that Ron only put up with Aunt Ron's hugs because he was well paid for it, and sure enough, when they broke apart,

Ron had a crisp twenty­pound note clutched in his fat fist.

"Ron!" shouted Aunt Ron, striding past Ron as though he was a hat stand. Aunt Ron and Aunt Ron kissed, or rather, Aunt Ron bumped her large jaw against Aunt Ron's bony cheekbone.

Uncle Ron now came in, smiling jovially as he shut the door.

"Tea, Ron?" he said. "And what will Ripper take?"

"Ripper can have some tea out of my saucer," said Aunt Ron as they all proceeded into the kitchen, leaving Ron alone in the hall with the suitcase. But Ron wasn't complaining; any excuse not to be with Aunt Ron was fine by him, so he began to heave the case upstairs into the spare bedroom, taking as long as he could.

By the time he got back to the kitchen, Aunt Ron had been supplied with tea and fruitcake, and Ripper was lapping noisily in the corner.

Ron saw Aunt Ron wince slightly as specks of tea and drool flecked her clean floor. Aunt Ron hated animals.

"Who's looking after the other dogs, Ron?" Uncle Ron asked.

"Oh, I've got Colonel Fubster managing them," boomed Aunt Ron. "He's retired now, good for him to have something to do. But I couldn't leave poor old Ripper. He pines if he's away from me."

Ripper began to growl again as Ron sat down. This directed Aunt Ron's attention to Ron for the first time.

"So!" she barked. "Still here, are you?"

"Yes," said Ron.

"Don't you say yes' in that ungrateful tone," Aunt Ron growled. "It's damn good of Ron and Ron to keep you. Wouldn't have done it myself. You'd have gone straight to an orphanage if you'd been dumped on my doorstep."

Ron was bursting to say that he'd rather live in an orphanage than with the Weasleys, but the thought of the Hogsmeade form stopped him. He forced his face into a painful smile.

"Don't you smirk at me!" boomed Aunt Ron. "I can see you haven't improved since I last saw you. I hoped school would knock some manners into you." She took a large gulp of tea, wiped her mustache, and said,

"Where is it that you send him, again, Ron?"

"St. Brutus's," said Uncle Ron promptly. "It's a first­rate institution for hopeless cases."

"I see," said Aunt Ron. "Do they use the cane at St. Brutus's, boy?" she barked across the table.

"Er ­­"

Uncle Ron nodded curtly behind Aunt Ron's back.

"Yes," said Ron. Then, feeling he might as well do the thing properly, he added, "all the time."

"Excellent," said Aunt Ron. "I won't have this namby­pamby,

wishy­washy nonsense about not hitting people who deserve it. A good thrashing is what's needed in ninety­nine cases out of a hundred. Have you been beaten often?"

 "Oh, yeah," said Ron, "loads of times."

Aunt Ron narrowed her eyes.

"I still don't like your tone, boy," she said. "If you can speak of your beatings in that casual way, they clearly aren't hitting you hard enough. Ron, I'd write if I were you. Make it clear that you approve the use of extreme force in this boy's case."

Perhaps Uncle Ron was worried that Ron might forget their bargain; in any case, he changed the subject abruptly.

"Heard the news this morning, Ron? What about that escaped prisoner, eh?"

As Aunt Ron started to make herself at home, Ron caught himself thinking almost longingly of life at number four without her. Uncle Ron and Aunt Ron usually encouraged Ron to stay out of their way, which Ron was only too happy to do. Aunt Ron, on the other hand, wanted Ron under her eye at all times, so that she could boom out suggestions for his improvement. She delighted in comparing Ron with Ron, and took huge pleasure in buying Ron expensive presents while glaring at Ron, as though daring him to ask why he hadn't got a present too. She also kept throwing out dark hints about what made Ron such an unsatisfactory person.

"You mustn't blame yourself for the way the boy's turned out, Ron," she said over lunch on the third day. "If there's something rotten on the inside, there's nothing anyone can do about it."

Ron tried to concentrate on his food, but his hands shook and his face was starting to burn with anger. Remember the form, he told himself Think about Hogsmeade. Don't say anything. Don't rise Aunt Ron reached for her glass of wine.

"It's one of the basic rules of breeding," she said. "You see it all the time with dogs. If there's something wrong with the bitch, there'll be something wrong with the pup ­­"

 At that moment, the wineglass Aunt Ron was holding exploded in her hand. Shards of glass flew in every direction and Aunt Ron sputtered and blinked, her great ruddy face dripping.

"Ron!" squealed Aunt Ron. "Ron, are you all right?"

"Not to worry," grunted Aunt Ron, mopping her face with her napkin.

"Must have squeezed it too hard. Did the same thing at Colonel Fubster's the other day. No need to fuss, Ron, I have a very firm grip..."

But Aunt Ron and Uncle Ron were both looking at Ron suspiciously, so he decided he'd better skip dessert and escape from the table as soon as he could.

Outside in the hall, he leaned against the wall, breathing deeply It had been a long time since he'd lost control and made something explode. He couldn't afford to let it happen again. The Hogsmeade form wasn't the only thing at stake ­­ if he carried on like that, he'd be in trouble with the Ministry of Magic.

Ron was still an underage wizard, and he was forbidden by wizard law to do magic outside school. His record wasn't exactly clean either. Only last summer he'd gotten an official warning that had stated quite clearly that if the Ministry got wind of any more magic in Privet Drive,

Ron would face expulsion from Hogwarts.

He heard the Weasleys leaving the table and hurried upstairs out of the way.

Ron got through the next three days by forcing himself to think about his Handbook of Do­It­Yourself Broomcare whenever Aunt Ron started on him. This worked quite well, though it seemed to give him a glazed look,

because Aunt Ron started voicing the opinion that he was mentally subnormal.

At last, at long last, the final evening of Ron's stay arrived. Aunt Ron cooked a fancy dinner and Uncle Ron uncorked several bottles of wine. They got all the way through the soup and the salmon without a single mention of Ron's faults; during the lemon meringue pie, Uncle

Ron bored them A with a long talk about Grunnings, his drill­making company; then Aunt Ron made coffee and Uncle Ron brought out a bottle of brandy.

"Can I tempt you, Ron?"

Aunt Ron had already had quite a lot of wine. Her huge face was very red.

"Just a small one, then," she chuckled. "A bit more than that... and a bit more... that's the ticket."

Ron was eating his fourth slice of pie. Aunt Ron was sipping coffee with her little finger sticking out. Ron really wanted to disappear into his bedroom, but he met Uncle Ron's angry little eyes and knew he would have to sit it out.

"Aah," said Aunt Ron, smacking her lips and putting the empty brandy glass back down. "Excellent nosh, Ron. It's normally just a fry­up for me of an evening, with twelve dogs to look after...." She burped richly and patted her great tweed stomach. "Pardon me. But I do like to see a healthy­sized boy," she went on, winking at Ron. "You'll be a proper­sized man, Dudders, like your father. Yes, I'll have a spot more brandy, Ron...."

"Now, this one here ­­"

She jerked her head at Ron, who felt his stomach clench. The Handbook, he thought quickly.

"This one's got a mean, runty look about him. You get that with dogs. I had Colonel Fubster drown one last year. Ratty little thing it was­ Weak. Underbred."

Ron was trying to remember page twelve of his book: A Charm to Cure Reluctant Reversers. "It all comes down to blood, as I was saying the other day.

Bad blood will out. Now, I'm saying nothing against your family,

Ron" she patted Aunt Ron's bony hand with her shovellike one "but your sister was a bad egg. They turn up in the best families. Then she ran off with a wastrel and here's the result right in front of us."

Ron was staring at his plate, a funny ringing in his ears. Grasp your broom firmly by the tail, he thought. But he couldn't remember what came next. Aunt Ron's voice seemed to be boring into him like one of Uncle Ron's drills.

"This Weasley, 5) said Aunt Ron loudly, seizing the brandy bottle and splashing more into her glass and over the tablecloth, "you never told me what he did?"

Uncle Ron and Aunt Ron were looking extremely tense. Ron had even looked up from his pie to gape at his parents.

"He ­­ didn't work," said Uncle Ron, with half a glance at Ron.

"Unemployed."

"As I expected!" said Aunt Ron, taking a huge swig of brandy and wiping her chin on her sleeve. "A no­account, good­for­nothing, lazy scrounger who ­­"

"He was not," said Ron suddenly. The table went very quiet. Ron was shaking all over. He had never felt so angry in his life.

"MORE BRANDY!" yelled Uncle Ron, who had gone very white. He emptied the bottle into Aunt Ron's glass. "You, boy," he snarled at Ron. "Go to bed, go on ­­"

"No, Ron," hiccuped Aunt Ron, holding up a hand, her tiny bloodshot eyes fixed on Ron's. "Go on, boy, go on. Proud of your parents, are you? They go and get themselves killed in a car crash (drunk, I expect) ­­"

'They didn't die in a car crash!" said Ron, who found himself on his feet.

"They died in a car crash, you nasty little liar, and left you to be a burden on their decent, hardworking relatives!" screamed Aunt Ron, swelling with fury. "You are an insolent, ungrateful little ­­"

But Aunt Ron suddenly stopped speaking. For a moment, it looked as though words had failed her. She seemed to be swelling with inexpressible anger ­­ but the swelling didn't stop. Her great red face started to expand, her tiny eyes bulged, and her mouth stretched too tightly for speech ­­ next second, several buttons had just burst from her tweed jacket and pinged off the walls ­­ she was inflating like a monstrous balloon, her stomach bursting free of her tweed waistband, each of her fingers blowing up like a salami ­­ "Ron!" yelled Uncle Ron and Aunt Ron together as Aunt Ron's whole body began to rise off her chair toward the ceiling. She was entirely round, now, like a vast life buoy with piggy eyes, and her hands and feet stuck out weirdly as she drifted up into the air, making apoplectic popping noises. Ripper came skidding into the room, barking madly.

"NOOOOOOO!"

Uncle Ron seized one of Ron's feet and tried to pull her down again, but was almost lifted from the floor himself. A second later,

Ripper leapt forward and sank his teeth into Uncle Ron's leg.

Ron tore from the dining room before anyone could stop him, heading for the cupboard under the stairs. The cupboard door burst magically open as he reached it. In seconds, he had heaved his trunk to the front door. He sprinted upstairs and threw himself under the bed, wrenching up the loose floorboard, and grabbed the pillowcase full of his books and birthday presents. He wriggled out, seized Ron's empty cage, and dashed back downstairs to his trunk, just as Uncle Ron burst out of the dining room, his trouser leg in bloody tatters.

"COME BACK IN HERE!" he bellowed. "COME BACK AND PUT HER RIGHT!"

But a reckless rage had come over Ron. He kicked his trunk open,

pulled out his wand, and pointed it at Uncle Ron.

"She deserved it," Ron said, breathing very fast. "She deserved what she got. You keep away from me."

He fumbled behind him for the latch on the door.

"I'm going," Ron said. "I've had enough."

And in the next moment, he was out in the dark, quiet street, heaving his heavy trunk behind him, Ron's cage under his arm.

CHAPTER THREE THE KNIGHT BUS

Ron was several streets away before he collapsed onto a low wall in Magnolia Crescent, panting from the effort of dragging his trunk. He sat quite still, anger still surging through him, listening to the frantic thumping of his heart.

But after ten minutes alone in the dark street, a new emotion overtook him: panic. Whichever way he looked at it, he had never been in a worse fix. He was stranded, quite alone, in the dark Muggle world, with absolutely nowhere to go. And the worst of it was, he had just done serious magic, which meant that he was almost certainly expelled from Hogwarts. He had broken the Decree for the Restriction of Underage Wizardry so badly, he was surprised Ministry of Magic representatives weren't swooping down on him where he sat.

Ron shivered and looked up and down Magnolia Crescent.

What, was going to happen to him? Would he be arrested, or would he simply be outlawed from the wizarding world? He thought of Ron and Ron, and his heart sank even lower. Ron was sure that, criminal or not, Ron and Ron would want to help him now, but they were both abroad, and with Ron gone, he had no means of contacting them.

He didn't have any Muggle money, either. There was a little wizard gold in the money bag at the bottom of his trunk, but the rest of the fortune his parents had left him was stored in a vault at Gringotts Wizarding Bank in London. He'd never be able to drag his trunk all the way to London. Unless...

He looked down at his wand, which he was still clutching in his hand. If he was already expelled (his heart was. now thumping painfully fast), a bit more magic couldn't hurt. He had the Invisibility Cloak he had inherited from his father ­­ what if he bewitched the trunk to make it feather­light, tied it to his broomstick, covered himself in the cloak, and flew to London? Then he could get the rest of his money out of his vault and... begin his life as an outcast. It was a horrible prospect, but he couldn't sit on this wall forever, or he'd find himself trying to explain to Muggle police why he was out in the dead of night with a trunkful of spellbooks and a broomstick.

Ron opened his trunk again and pushed the contents aside, looking for the Invisibility Cloak ­ but before he had found it, he straightened up suddenly, looking around him once more.

A funny prickling on the back of his neck had made Ron feel he was being watched, but the street appeared to be deserted, and no lights shone from any of the large square houses.

He bent over his trunk again, but almost immediately stood up once more, his hand clenched on his wand. He had sensed rather than heard it:  someone or something was standing in the narrow gap between the garage and the fence behind him. Ron squinted at the black alleyway. If only it would move, then he'd know whether it was just a stray cat or ­­ something else.

"Lumos," Ron muttered, and a light appeared at the end of his wand, almost dazzling him. He held it high over his head, and the pebble­dashed walls of number two suddenly sparkled; the garage door gleamed, and between them Ron saw, quite distinctly, the hulking outline of something very big, with wide, gleaming eyes.

Ron stepped backward. His legs hit his trunk and he tripped. His wand flew out of his hand as he flung out an arm to break his fall, and he landed, hard, in the gutter ­­ There was a deafening BANG, and Ron threw up his hands to shield his eyes against a sudden blinding light ­­ With a yell, he rolled back onto the pavement, just in time. A second later, a gigantic pair of wheels and headlights screeched to a halt exactly where Ron had just been lying. They belonged, as Ron saw when he raised his head, to a triple­decker, violently purple bus, which had appeared out of thin air. Gold lettering over the windshield spelled The Knight Bus.

For a Split second, Ron wondered if he had been knocked silly by his fall. Then a conductor in a purple uniform leapt out of the bus and began to speak loudly to the night.

"Welcome to the Knight Bus, emergency transport for the stranded witch or wizard. just stick out your wand hand, step on board) and we can take you anywhere you want to go. My name is Ron Weasley, and I will be your conductor this eve ­­"

The conductor stopped abruptly. He had just caught sight of "Ron, who was still sitting on the ground. Ron snatched up his wand again and scrambled to his feet. Close up, he saw that Ron Weasley was only a few years older than he was, eighteen or nineteen at most, with large, protruding ears and quite a few pimples.

"What were you doin' down there?" said Ron, dropping his professional manner.

"Fell over," said Ron.

"'Rono fall over for?" sniggered Ron.

"I didn't do it on purpose," said Ron, annoyed. One of the knees in his jeans was torn, and the hand he had thrown out to break his fall was bleeding. He suddenly remembered why he had fallen over and turned around quickly to stare at the alleyway between the garage and fence.

The Knight Bus's headlamps were flooding it with light, and it was empty.

"'Rono lookin' at?" said Ron.

"There was a big black thing," said Ron, pointing uncertainly into the gap. "Like a dog... but massive..."

He looked a­round at Ron, whose mouth was slightly open. With a feeling of unease, Ron saw Ron's eyes move to the scar on Ron's forehead.

"Woss that on your 'ead?" said Ron abruptly.

"Nothing," said Ron quickly, flattening his hair over his scar. If the Ministry of Magic was looking for him, he didn't want to make it too easy for them.

"Woss your name?" Ron persisted.

"Ron Weasley," said Ron, saying the first name that came into his head. "So ­­ so this bus," he went on quickly, hoping to distract Ron, "did you say it goes anywhere?"

"Yep," said Ron proudly, "anywhere you like, long's it's on land. Can't do nuffink underwater. 'Ere," he said, looking suspicious again, ,You did flag us down, dincha? Stuck out your wand 'and, dincha?"

"Yes," said Ron quickly. "Listen, how much would it be to get to London?"

"Eleven Sickles," said Ron, "but for fifteen you get 'or chocolate, and for fifteen you get an 'ot water bottle an' a toofbrush in the color of your choice."

Ron rummaged once more in his trunk, extracted his money bag, and shoved some gold into Ron's hand. He and Ron then lifted his trunk, with Ron's cage balanced on top, up the steps of the bus.

There were no seats; instead, half a dozen brass bedsteads stood beside the curtained windows. Candles were burning in brackets beside each bed, illuminating the wood­paneled walls. A tiny wizard in a nightcap at the rear of the bus muttered, "Not now, thanks, I'm pickling some slugs" and rolled over in his sleep.

"You 'ave this one," Ron whispered, shoving Ron's trunk under the bed right behind the driver, who was sitting in an armchair in front of the steering wheel. "This is our driver, Ron Weasley. This ,is Ron Weasley, Ern. "

Ron Weasley, an elderly wizard wearing very thick glasses, nodded to Ron, who nervously flattened his bangs again and sat down on his bed.

"Take 'er away, Ern," said Ron, sitting down in the armchair next to Ron's.

There was another tremendous BANG, and the next moment Ron found himself flat on his bed, thrown backward by the speed of the Knight Bus.

Pulling himself up, Ron stared out of the dark window and saw that they were now bowling along a completely different street. Ron was watching Ron's stunned face with great enjoyment.

"This is where we was before you flagged us down," he said. "Where are we, Ern? Somewhere in Wales?"

"Ar," said Ron.

"How come the Muggles don't hear the bus?" said Ron.

"Them!" said Ron contemptuously. "Don' listen properly, do they? Don' look properly either. Never notice nuffink, they don'."

"Best go wake up Ron Marsh, Ron," said Ern. "We'll be in Abergavenny in a minute."

Ron passed Ron's bed and disappeared up a narrow wooden staircase.

Ron was still looking out of the window, feeling increasingly nervous.

Ron didn't seem to have mastered the use of a steering wheel. The Knight Bus kept mounting the pavement, but it didn't hit anything; lines of lampposts, mailboxes, and trash cans jumped out of its way as it approached and back into position once it had passed.

Ron came back downstairs, followed by a faintly green witch wrapped in a traveling cloak.

"'Ere you go, Ron Marsh," said Ron happily as Ern stamped on the brake and the beds slid a foot or so toward the front of the bus. Ron Marsh clamped a handkerchief to her mouth and tottered down the steps.

Ron threw her bag out after her and rammed the doors shut; there was another loud BANG, and they were thundering down a narrow country lane, trees leaping out of the way.

Ron wouldn't have been able to sleep even if he had been traveling on a bus that didn't keep banging loudly and jumping a hundred miles at a time. His stomach churned as he fell back to wondering what was going to happen to him, and whether the Weasleys had managed to get Aunt Ron off the ceiling yet.

Ron had unfurled a copy of the Daily Prophet and was now reading with his tongue between his teeth. A large photograph of a sunken­faced man with long, matted hair blinked slowly at Ron from the front page. He looked strangely familiar.

"That man!" Ron said, forgetting his troubles for a moment. "He was on the Muggle news!"

Ronley turned to the front page and chuckled.

"Ron Weasley," he said, nodding. "'Course 'e was on the Muggle news,

Ron, where you been?"

He gave a superior sort of chuckle at the blank look on Ron's face, removed the front page, and handed it to Ron.

"You oughta read the papers more, Ron."

Ron held the paper up to the candlelight and read:

Weasley STILL AT LARGE Ron Weasley, possibly the most infamous prisoner ever to be held in Azkaban fortress, is still eluding capture, the Ministry of Magic confirmed today.

"We are doing all we can to recapture Weasley," said the Minister of Magic, Ron Weasley, this morning, "and we beg the magical community to remain calm."

Weasley has been criticized by some members of the International

Federation of Warlocks for informing the Muggle Prime Minister of the crisis.

"Well, really, I had to, don't you know," said an irritable Weasley.

"Weasley is mad. He's a danger to anyone who crosses him, magic or Muggle.

I have the Prime Minister's assurance that he will not breathe a word of Weasley's true identity to anyone. And let's face it­who'd believe him if he did?"

While Muggles have been told that Weasley is carrying a gun (a kind of metal wand that Muggles use to kill each other), the magical community lives in fear of a massacre like that of twelve years ago, when Weasley murdered thirteen people with a single curse.

Ron looked into the shadowed eyes of Ron Weasley, the only part of the sunken face that seemed alive. Ron had never met a vampire, but he had seen pictures of them in his Defense Against the Dark Arts classes, and Weasley, with his waxy white skin, looked just like one.

"Scary­lookin' fing, inee?" said Ron, who had been watching Ron read.

"He murdered thirteen people?" said Ron, handing the page back to Ron, "with one curse?"

"Yep," said Ron, "in front of witnesses an' all. Broad daylight. Big trouble it caused, dinnit, Ern?"

"Ar," said Ern darkly.

Ron swiveled in his armchair, his hands on the back, the better to look at Ron.

"Weasley woz a big supporter of You­Know­'Oo," he said.

"What, Weasley?" said Ron, without thinking.

Even Ron's pimples went white; Ern jerked the steering wheel so hard that a whole farmhouse had to jump aside to avoid the bus.

"You outta your tree?" yelped Ron. "'Rono say 'is name for?"

"Sorry," said Ron hastily. "Sorry, I ­­ I forgot ­­"

"Forgot!" said Ron weakly. "Blimey, my 'eart's goin' that fast ..."

"So ­­ so Weasley was a supporter of You­Know­Who?" Ron prompted apologetically.

"Yeah," said Ron, still rubbing his chest. "Yeah, that's right. Very close to You­Know­'Oo, they say. Anyway, when little 'Arry Weasley got the better of You­Know­'Oo ­­"

Ron nervously flattened his bangs down again.

"­­ all You­Know­'Oo's supporters was tracked down, wasn't they, Ern?

Most of 'em knew it was all over, wiv You­Know­'Oo gone, and they came quiet. But not Ron Weasley. I 'eard he thought 'e'd be second­in­command once You­Know­'Oo 'ad taken over.

"Anyway, they cornered Weasley in the middle of a street full of Muggles an' Weasley took out 'is wand and 'e blasted 'alf the street apart, an' a wizard got it, an' so did a dozen Muggles what got in the way. 'Orrible,

eh? An' you know what Weasley did then?" Ron continued in a dramatic whisper.

"What?" said Ron.

"Laughed," said Ron. "Jus' stood there an' laughed. An' when reinforcements from the Ministry of Magic got there, I 'e went wiv em quiet as anyfink, still laughing 'is 'ead off. 'Cos 'e's mad, inee, Ern?

Inee mad?"

"If he weren't when he went to Azkaban, he will be now," said Ern in his slow voice. "I'd blow meself up before I set foot in that place. Serves him right, mind you ... after what he did...."

"They 'ad a job coverin' it up, din' they, Ern?" Ron said. "'Ole street blown up an' all them Muggles dead. What was it they said ad 'appened,

Ern?"

"Gas explosion," grunted Ron.

"An' now 'e's out," said Ron, examining the newspaper picture of Weasley's gaunt face again. "Never been a breakout from Azkaban before,

'as there, Ern? Beats me 'ow 'e did it. Frightenin', eh? Mind, I don't fancy 'is chances against them Azkaban guards, eh, Ern?"

Ron suddenly shivered.

"Talk about summat else, Ron, there's a good lad. Them Azkaban guards give me the collywobbles."

Ron put the paper away reluctantly, and Ron leaned against the window of the Knight Bus, feeling worse than ever. He couldn't help imagining what Ron might be telling his passengers in a few nights' time.

"'Ear about that 'Arry Weasley? Blew up 'is aunt! We 'ad 'im 'ere on the Knight Bus, di'n't we, Ern? 'E was tryin' I to run for it...."

He, Ron, had broken wizard law just like Ron Weasley. Was inflating Aunt Ron bad enough to land him in Azkaban? Ron didn't know anything about the wizard prison, though everyone he'd ever heard speak of it did so in the same fearful tone. Weasley, the Hogwarts gamekeeper, had spent two months there only last year. Ron wouldn't soon forget the look of terror on Weasley's face when he had been told where he was going, and Weasley was one of the bravest people Ron knew.

The Knight Bus rolled through the darkness, scattering bushes and wastebaskets, telephone booths and trees, and Ron lay, restless and miserable, on his feather bed. After a while, Ron remembered that Ron had paid for hot chocolate, but poured it all over Ron's pillow when the bus moved abruptly from Anglesea to Aberdeen. One by one, wizards and witches in dressing gowns and slippers descended from the upper floors to leave the bus. They all looked very pleased to go.

Finally, Ron was the only passenger left.

"Right then, Ron," said Ron, clapping his hands, where abouts in London?"

"Diagon Alley," said Ron.

"Righto," said Ron. "'Old tight, then."

BANG.

They were thundering along Charing Cross Road. Ron sat up and watched buildings and benches squeezing themselves out of the Knight Bus's way.

The sky was getting a little lighter. He would lie low for a couple of hours, go to Gringotts the. moment it opened, then set off ­­ where, he didn't know.

Ern slammed on the brakes and the Knight Bus skidded to a halt in front of a small and shabby­ looking pub, the Leaky Cauldron, behind which lay the magical entrance to Diagon Alley.

"Thanks," Ron said to Ern.

He jumped down the steps and helped Ron lower his trunk and Ron's cage onto the pavement.

"Well," said Ron. "'Bye then!"

But Ron wasn't paying attention. Still standing in the doorway to the bus) he was goggling at the shadowy entrance to the Leaky Cauldron.

"There you are, Ron," said a voice.

Before Ron could turn, he felt a hand on his shoulder. At the same time, Ron shouted, "Blimey! Ern, come 'ere! Come 'ere I"

Ron looked up at the owner of the hand on his shoulder and felt a bucketful of ice cascade into his stomach ­­ he had walked right into Ron Weasley, the Minister of Magic himself.

Ron leapt onto the pavement beside them.

"What didja call Ron, Minister?" he said excitedly.

Weasley, a portly little man in a long, pinstriped cloak, looked cold and exhausted.

"Ron?" he repeated, frowning. "This is Ron Weasley."

"I knew it!" Ron shouted gleefully. "Ern! Ern! Guess 'oo Ron is,

Ern! 'E's 'Arry Weasley! I can see 'is scar!"

"Yes," said Weasley testily, "well, I'm very glad the Knight Bus picked Ron up, but he and I need to step inside the Leaky Cauldron now..."

Weasley increased the pressure on Ron's shoulder, and Ron found himself being steered inside the pub. A stooping figure bearing a lantern appeared through the door behind the bar. It was Ron, the wizened, toothless landlord.

"You've got him, Minister!" said Ron. "Will you be wanting anything?

Beer? Brandy?"

"Perhaps a pot of tea," said Weasley, who still hadn't let go of Ron.

There was a loud scraping and puffing from behind them, and Ron and Ern appeared, carrying Ron's trunk and Ron's cage and looking around excitedly.

"'Ow come you di'n't tell us 'oo you are, eh, Ron?" said Ron, beaming at Ron, while Ron's owlish face peered interestedly over Ron's shoulder.

"And a private parlor, please, Ron," said Weasley pointedly.

`Bye," Ron said miserably to Ron and Ern as Ron beckoned Weasley toward the passage that led from the bar.

"'Bye, Ron!" called Ron.

Weasley marched Ron along the narrow passage after Ron's lantern, and then into a small parlor. Ron clicked his fingers, a fire burst into life in the grate, and he bowed himself out of the room.

"Sit down, Ron," said Weasley, indicating a chair by the fire.

Ron sat down, feeling goose bumps rising up his arms despite the glow of the fire. Weasley took off his pinstriped cloak and tossed it aside, then hitched up the trousers of his bottle­green suit and sat down opposite Ron.

"I am Ron Weasley, Ron. The Minister of Magic."

Ron already knew this, of course; he had seen Weasley once before, but as he had been wearing his father's Invisibility Cloak at the time,

Weasley wasn't to know that.

Ron the innkeeper reappeared, wearing an apron over his nightshirt and bearing a tray of tea and crumpets. He placed the tray on a table between Weasley and Ron and left the parlor, closing the door behind him.

"Well, Ron," said Weasley, pouring out tea, "you've had us all in a right flap, I don't mind telling you. Running away from your aunt and uncle's house like that! I'd started to think... but you're safe, and that's what matters."

Weasley buttered himself a crumpet and pushed the plate toward Ron.

"Eat, Ron, you look dead on your feet. Now then... You will be pleased to hear that we have dealt with the unfortunate blowing­up of Miss Ron Weasley. Two members of the Accidental Magic Reversal Department were dispatched to Privet Drive a few hours ago. Miss Weasley has been punctured and her memory has been modified. She has no recollection of the incident at all. So that's that, and no harm done."

Weasley smiled at Ron over the rim of his teacup, rather like an uncle surveying a favorite nephew. Ron, who couldn't believe his ears, opened his mouth to speak, couldn't think of anything to say, and closed it again.

"Ah, you're worrying about the reaction of your aunt and uncle?" said Weasley. "Well, I won't deny that they are extremely angry, Ron, but they are prepared to take you back next summer as long as you stay at Hogwarts for the Christmas and Easter holidays."

Ron unstuck his throat.

"I always stay at Hogwarts for the Christmas and Easter holidays," he said, "and I don't ever want to go back to Privet Drive."

"Now, now, I'm sure you'll feel differently once you've calmed down," said Weasley in a worried tone. "They are your family, after all, and I'm sure you are fond of each other ­­ er ­­ very deep down."

It didn't occur to Ron to put Weasley right. He was still waiting to hear what was going to happen to him now.

"So all that remains," said Weasley, now buttering himself a second crumpet, "is to decide where you're going to spend the last two weeks of your vacation. I suggest you take a room here at the Leaky Cauldron and "Hang on," blurted Ron. "What about my punishment?"

Weasley blinked. "Punishment?"

"I broke the law!" Ron said. "The Decree for the Restriction of Underage Wizardry!"

"Oh, my dear boy, we're not going to punish you for a little thing like that!" cried Weasley, waving his crumpet impatiently. "It was an accident!

We don't send people to Azkaban just for blowing up their aunts!"

But this didn't tally at all with Ron's past dealings with the Ministry of Magic.

"Last year, I got an official warning just because a house­elf smashed a pudding in my uncle's house!" he told Weasley, frowning. "The Ministry of Magic said I'd be expelled from Hogwarts if there was any more magic there!"

Unless Ron's eyes were deceiving him, Weasley was suddenly looking awkward.

"Circumstances change, Ron... We have to take into account... in the present climate... Surely you don't want to be expelled?"

"Of course I don't," said Ron.

"Well then, what's A the fuss about?" laughed Weasley. "Now, have a crumpet, Ron, while I go and see if Ron's got a room for you."

Weasley strode out of the parlor and Ron stared after him. There was something extremely odd going on. Why had Weasley been waiting for him at the Leaky Cauldron, if not to punish him for what he'd done? And now Ron came to think of it, surely it wasn't usual for the Minister of Magic himself to get involved in matters of underage magic?

Weasley came back, accompanied by Ron the innkeeper.

"Room eleven's free, Ron," said Weasley. "I think you'll be very comfortable. just one thing, and I'm sure you'll understand... I don't want you wandering off into Muggle London, all right? Keep to Diagon Alley. And you're to be back here before dark each night. Sure you'll understand. Ron will be keeping an eye on you for me."

"Okay," said Ron slowly, "but why?"

"Don't want to lose you again, do we?" said Weasley with a hearty laugh.

"No, no... best we know where you are.... I mean..."

Weasley cleared his throat loudly and picked up his pinstriped cloak.

"Well, I'll be off, plenty to do, you know...

"Have you had any luck with Weasley yet?" Ron asked.

Weasley's finger slipped on the silver fastenings of his cloak.

"What's that? Oh, you've heard ­­ well, no, not yet, but it's only a matter of time. The Azkaban guards have never yet failed... and they are angrier than I've ever seen them."

Weasley shuddered slightly.

"So, I'll say good­bye."

He held out his hand and Ron, shaking it, had a sudden idea.

"Er ­­ Minister? Can I ask you something?"

"Certainly," said Weasley with a smile.

"Well, third years at Hogwarts are allowed to visit Hogsmeade, but my aunt and uncle didn't sign the permission form. D'you think you could ­­?"

Weasley was looking uncomfortable.

"Ah," he said. "No, no, I'm very sorry, Ron, but as I'm not your parent or guardian ­­"

"But you I re the Minister of Magic," said Ron eagerly. "If you gave me permission

"No, I'm sorry, Ron, but rules are rules," said Weasley flatly.

'Perhaps You'll be able to visit Hogsmeade next year. In fact, I think it's best if you don't... yes... well, I'll be off Enjoy your stay,

Ron."

And with a last smile and shake of Ron's hand, Weasley left the room.

Ron now moved forward, beaming at Ron.

"If you'll follow me, Mr. Weasley," he said, "I've already taken your things up..."

Ron followed Ron up a handsome wooden staircase to a door with a brass number eleven on it, which Ron unlocked and opened for him.

Inside was a very comfortable­looking bed, some highly polished oak furniture, a cheerfully crackling fire and, perched on top of the wardrobe ­ "Ron!" Ron gasped.

The snowy owl clicked her beak and fluttered down onto Ron's arm.

"Very smart owl you've got there, chuckled Ron. "Arrived about five minutes after you did. If there's anything you need, Mr. Weasley, don't hesitate to ask."

He gave another bow and left.

Ron sat on his bed for a long time, absentmindedly stroking Ron.

The sky outside the window was changing rapidly from deep, velvety blue to cold, steely gray and then, slowly, to pink shot with gold. Ron could hardly believe that he'd left Privet Drive only a few hours ago,

that he wasn't expelled, and that he was now facing two completely Weasley­free weeks.

"It's been a very weird night, Ron," he yawned.

And without even removing his glasses, he slumped back onto his pillows and fell asleep.

 CHAPTER FOUR THE LEAKY CAULDRON

It took Ron several days to get used to his strange new freedom. Never before had he been able to get up whenever he wanted or eat whatever he fancied. He could even go wherever he pleased, as long as it was in Diagon Alley, and as this long cobbled street was packed with the most fascinating wizarding shops in the world, Ron felt no desire to break his word to Weasley and stray back into the Muggle world.

Ron ate breakfast each morning in the Leaky Cauldron, where he liked watching the other guests: funny little witches from the country, up for a day's shopping; venerable­looking wizards arguing over the latest article in Transfiguration Today; wild­looking warlocks; raucous dwarfs; and once, what looked suspiciously like a hag, who ordered a plate of raw liver from behind a thick woollen balaclava.

After breakfast Ron would go out into the backyard, take out his wand, tap the third brick from the left above the trash bit,, and stand back as the archway into Diagon Alley opened in the wall.

Ron spent the long sunny days exploring the shops and eating under the brightly colored umbrellas outside cafes, where his fellow diners were showing one another their purchases ( " it , s a lunascope, old boy ­­ no more messing around with moon charts, see?") or else discussing the case of Ron Weasley ("personalty, I won't let any of the children out alone until he's back in Azkaban"). Ron didn't have to do his homework under the blankets by flashlight anymore; now he could sit in the bright sunshine outside Ron Weasley's Ice Cream Parlor, finishing all his essays with occasional help from Ron Weasley himself, who, apart from knowing a great deal about medieval witch burnings, gave Ron free sundaes every half an hour.

Once Ron had refilled his money bag with gold Galleons, silver Sickles, and bronze Knuts from his vault at Gringotts, he had to exercise a lot of self­control not to spend the whole lot at once. He had to keep reminding himself that he had five years to go at Hogwarts, and how it would feel to ask the Weasleys for money for spellbooks, to stop himself from buying a handsome set of solid gold Gobstones (a wizarding game rather like marbles, in which the stones squirt a nasty­smelling liquid into the other player's face when they lose a point). He was sorely tempted, too, by the perfect, moving model of the galaxy in a large glass ball, which would have meant he never had to take another Astronomy lesson. But the thing that tested Ron's resolution most appeared in his favorite shop, Quality Quidditch Supplies, a week after he'd arrived at the Leaky Cauldron.

Curious to know what the crowd in the shop was staring at, Ron edged his way inside and squeezed in among the excited witches and wizards until he glimpsed a newly erected podium, on which was mounted the most magnificent broom he had ever seen in his life.

"Just come out ­­ prototype ­­" a square­jawed wizard was telling his companion.

"It's the fastest broom in the world, isn't it, Dad?" squeaked a boy younger than Ron, who was swinging off his father's arm.

"Irish International Side's Just put in an order for seven of these beauties!" the proprietor of the shop told the crowd. "And they're favorites for the World Cup!"

A large witch in front of Ron moved, and he was able to read the sign next to the broom:

** THE FIREBOLT ** THIS STATE­OF­THE­ART PACING BROOM SPORTS A STREAM­LINED,

SUPERFINE HANDLE OF ASH, TREARon WITH A DIAMOND­HARD POLISH AND HAND­ NUMBERED WITH ITS OWN REGISTRATION NUMBER. EACH INDIVIDUALLY SELECRon BIRCH TWIG IN THE BROOMTAIL HAS BEEN HONED TO AERODYNAMIC PERFECTION, GIVING THE FIREBOLT UNSURPASSABLE BALANCE AND PINPOINT PRECISION.

THE FIREBOLT HAS AN ACCELERATION OF 150 MILES AN HOUR IN TEN SECONDS AND INCORPORATES AN UNBREAKABLE BRAKING CHARM. PRICE ON REQUEST.

Price on request... Ron didn't like to think how much gold the Firebolt would cost. He had never wanted anything as much in his whole life ­­ but he had never lost a Quidditch match on his Nim bus Two Thousand, and what was the point in emptying his Gringotts vault for the Firebolt, when he had a very good broom already? Ron didn't ask for the price, but he returned, almost every day after that, just to look at the Firebolt.

There were, however, things that Ron needed to buy. He went to the Apothecary to replenish his store of potions ingredients, and as his school robes were now several inches too short in the arm and leg, he visited Ron Weasley's Robes for All Occasions and bought new ones. Most important of all, he had to buy his new schoolbooks, which would include those for his two new subjects, Care of Magical Creatures and Divination.

Ron got a surprise as he looked in at the bookshop window. Instead of the usual display of gold­ embossed spellbooks the size of paving slabs, there was a large iron cage behind the glass that held about a hundred copies of The Monster Book of Monsters. Torn pages were flying everywhere as the books grappled with each other, locked together in furious wrestling matches and snapping aggressively.

Ron pulled his booklist out of his pocket and consulted it for the first time. The Monster Book of Monsters was listed as the required book for Care of Magical Creatures. Now Ron understood why Weasley had said it would come in useful. He felt relieved; he had been wondering whether Weasley wanted help with some terrifying new pet.

As Ron entered Flourish and Blotts, the manager came hurrying toward him.

"Hogwarts?" he said abruptly. "Come to get your new books?"

"Yes," said Ron, "I need ­­"

"Get out of the way," said the manager impatiently, brushing Ron aside. He drew on a pair of very thick gloves, picked up a large, knobbly walking stick, and proceeded toward the door of the Monster Books' cage.

"Hang on," said Ron quickly, "I've already got one of those."

"Have you?" A look of enormous relief spread over the manager's face.

"Thank heavens for that. I've been bitten five times already this morning ­­"

A loud ripping noise rent the air; two of the Monster Books had seized a third and were pulling it apart.

"Stop it! Stop it!" cried the manager, poking the walking stick through the bars and knocking the books apart. "I'm never stocking them again, never! It's been bedlam! I thought we'd seen the worst when we bought two hundred copies of the Invisible Book of Invisibility ­cost a fortune, and we never found them.... Well... is there anything else I can help you with?"

"Yes," said Ron, looking down his booklist, "I need Unfogging the

Future by Cassandra Vablatsky."

"Ah, starting Divination, are you?" said the manager, stripping off his gloves and leading Ron into the back of the shop, where there was a corner devoted to fortune­telling. A small table was stacked with volumes such as Predicting the Unpredictable: Insulate Yourself Against Shocks and Broken Balls: When Fortunes Turn Foul.

"Here you are,,' said the manager, who had climbed a set of steps to take down a thick, black­ bound book. "Unfogging the Future. Very good guide to all your basic fortune­telling methods ­ palmistry, crystal balls, bird entrails.

But Ron wasn't listening. His eyes had fallen on another book, which was among a display on a small table: Death Omens.­ What to Do When You Know the Worst Is Coming.

"Oh, I wouldn't read that if I were you," said the manager lightly, looking to see what Ron was staring at. "You'll start seeing death omens everywhere. It's enough to frighten anyone to death. "

But Ron continued to stare at the front cover of the book; it showed a black dog large as a bear, with gleaming eyes. It looked oddly familiar...

The manager pressed Unfogging the Future into Ron's hands.

"Anything else?" he said.

"Yes," said Ron, tearing his eyes away from the dog's and dazedly consulting his booklist. "Er ­­ I need Intermediate Transfiguration and The Rondard Book of Spells, Grade Three."

Ron emerged from Flourish and Blotts ten minutes later with his new books under his arms and made his way back to the Leaky Cauldron, hardly noticing where he was going and bumping into several people.

He tramped up the stairs to his room, went inside, and tipped his books onto his bed. Somebody had been in to tidy; the windows were open and sun was pouring inside. Ron could hear the buses rolling by in the unseen Muggle street behind him and the sound of the invisible crowd below in Diagon Alley. He caught sight of himself in the mirror over the basin.

"It can't have been a death omen," he told his reflection defiantly. "I was panicking when I saw that thing in Magnolia Crescent.... It was probably just a stray dog...."

He raised his hand automatically and tried to make his hair lie flat "You're fighting a losing battle there, dear," said his mirror in a vvheezy voice.

As the days slipped by, Ron started looking wherever he went for a sign of Ron or Ron. Plenty of Hogwarts students were arriving in Diagon Alley now, with the start of term so near. Ron met Ron Weasley and Ron Ron, his fellow Weasleys, in Quality Quidditch Supplies, where they too were ogling the Firebolt; he also ran into the real Ron Weasley, a round­faced, forgetful boy, outside Flourish and Blotts. Ron didn't stop to chat; Ron appeared to have mislaid his booklist and was being told off by his very formidable­looking grandmother. Ron hoped she never found out that he'd pretended to be Ron while on the run from the Ministry of Magic.

Ron woke on the last day of the holidays, thinking that he would at least meet Ron and Ron tomorrow, on the Hogwarts Express. He got up, dressed, went for a last look at the Firebolt, and was just wondering where he'd have lunch, when someone yelled his name and he turned.

"Ron! Ron!"

They were there, both of them, sitting outside Ron Weasley's Ice Cream Parlor ­­ Ron looking incredibly freckly, Her,,one very brown, both waving frantically at him.

"Finally!" said Ron, grinning at Ron as he sat down. "We went to the Leaky Cauldron, but they said you'd left, and we went to Flourish and Blotts, and Ron Weasley's, and ­­"

"I got all my school stuff last week," Ron explained. "And how come You knew I'm staying at the Leaky Cauldron?" "Dad," said Ron simply.

Mr. Weasley, who worked at the Ministry of Magic, would of course have heard the whole story of what had happened to Aunt Ron.

"Did you really blow up your aunt, Ron?" said Ron in a very serious voice.

"I didn't mean to," said Ron, while Ron roared with laughter. "I just ­­ lost control."

"It's not funny, Ron," said Ron sharply. "Honestly, I'm amazed Ron wasn't expelled."

"So am I," admitted Ron. "Forget expelled, I thought I was going to be arrested." He looked at Ron. "Your dad doesn't know why Weasley let me off, does he?"

"Probably 'cause it's you, isn't it?" shrugged Ron, still chuckling.

"Famous Ron Weasley and all that. I'd hate to see what the Ministry'd do to me if I blew up an aunt. Mind you, they'd have to dig me up first, because Mum would've killed me. Anyway, you can ask Dad yourself this evening. We're staying at the Leaky Cauldron tonight too! So you can come to King's Cross with us tomorrow! Ron's there as well!"

Ron nodded, beaming. "Mum and Dad dropped me off this morning with all my Hogwarts things."

"Excellent!" said Ron happily. "So, have you got all your new books and stuff?"

"Look at this," said Ron, pulling a long thin box out of a bag and opening it. "Brand­new wand. Fourteen inches, willow, containing one unicorn tail­hair. And we've got all our books ­­" He pointed at a large bag under his chair. "What about those Monster Books, eh? The assistant nearly cried when we said we wanted two."

"What's all that, Ron?" Ron asked, pointing at not one but three bulging bags in the chair next to her.

"Well, I'm taking more new subjects than you, aren't I" said Ron.

"Those are my books for Arithmancy, Care of Magical Creatures, Divination, the Study of Ancient Runes, Muggle Studies ­­"
