"What are you doing Muggle Studies for?" said Ron, rolling his eyes at Ron. "You're Muggle­ born! Your mum and dad are Muggles! You already know all about Muggles!"

"Are you planning to eat or sleep at all this year, Ron?" asked Ron, while Ron sniggered. Ron ignored them.

"I've still got ten Galleons," she said, checking her purse. "It's my birthday in September, and Mum and Dad gave me some money to get myself an early birthday present."

I mean, Ron's got Ron and you've got Ron ­­"

He pulled his pet rat out of his pocket. "And I want to get him checked over," he added, placing Ron on the table in front of them. "I don't think Egypt agreed with him."

Ron was looking thinner than usual, and there was a definite droop to his whiskers.

"There's a magical creature shop just over there," said Ron, who knew Diagon Alley very well by now. "You could see if they've got anything for Ron, and Ron can get her owl,"

So they paid for their ice cream and crossed the street to the Magical Menagerie.

There wasn't much room inside. Every inch of wall was hidden by cages.

It was smelly and very noisy because the occupants Of these cages were all squeaking, squawking, jabbering, or hissing. The witch behind the counter was already advising a wizard on the care of double­ended newts, so Ron, Ron, and Ron waited, examining the cages.

A pair of enormous purple toads sat gulping wetly and feasting on dead blowflies. A gigantic tortoise with a jewel­encrusted shell was glittering near the window. Poisonous orange snails were oozing slowly up the side of their glass tank, and a fat white rabbit kept changing into a silk top hat and back again with a loud popping noise. Then there were cats of every color, a noisy cage of ravens, a basket of funny custard­colored furballs that were humming loudly, and on the counter, a vast cage of sleek black rats that were playing some sort of skipping game using their long, bald tails.

The double­ended newt wizard left, and Ron approached the counter.

"It's my rat," he told the witch. "He been a bit off­color ever since I brought him back from Egypt."

"Bang him on the counter," said the witch, pulling a pair of heavy black spectacles out of her pocket.

Ron lifted Ron out of his inside pocket and placed him next to the cage of his fellow rats, who stopped their skipping tricks and scuffled to the wire for a better took.

Like nearly everything Ron owned, Ron the rat was secondhand (he had once belonged to Ron's brother Ron) and a bit battered. Next to the glossy rats in the cage, he looked especially woebegone.

"Hm," said the witch, picking up Ron. "How old is this rat?"

"Dunno," said Ron. "Quite old. He used to belong to my brother."

"Er ­­" The truth was that Ron had never shown the faintest trace of interesting powers. The witchs eyes moved from Ron's tattered

She indicated the black rats, who promptly started skipping again. Ron muttered, "Show­offs."

"Well, if you Don't want a replacement, you can try this rat tonic,"

said the witch, reaching under the counter and bringing out a small red bottle.

Ron buckled as something huge and orange came soaring from the top of the highest cage, landed on his head, and then propelled itself,

spitting madly, at Ron.

"Ron!" Ron shouted, racing out of the shop after him; Ron followed.

It took them nearly ten minutes to catch Ron, who had taken refuge under a wastepaper bin outside Quality Quidditch Supplies. Ron stuffed the trembling rat back into his pocket and straightened up, massaging his head.

"What was that?"

"It was either a very big cat or quite a small tiger," said Ron.

 "Where's Ron?"

"Probably getting her owl They made their way back up the crowded street to the Magical Menagerie.

That was a matter of opinion, thought Ron. The cat's ginger fur was thick and fluffy, but it was definitely a bit bowlegged and its face looked grumpy and oddly squashed, as though it had run headlong into a brick wall. Now that Ron was out of sight, however, the cat was purring contentedly in Ron's arms.

"Herinione, that thing nearly scalped me!" said Ron.

"Nor am 1, if it comes to that... but when you're dealing with a wizard like Weasley, you sometimes have to join forces with those you'd rather avoid."

"If they save Ron then I will never say another word against them,

said Mr. Weasley wearily. "It's late, Ron, we'd better go up...."

Ron heard chairs move. As quietly as he could, he hurried down the passage to the bar and out of sight. The parlor door opened, and a few seconds later footsteps told him that Mr. and Mrs. Weasley were climbing the stairs.

The bottle of rat tonic was lying under the table they had sat at earlier. Ron waited until he heard Mr. and Mrs. Weasley's bedroom door close, then headed back upstairs with the bottle.

Ron and Ron were crouching in the shadows on the landing, heaving with laughter as they listened to Ron dismantling his and Ron's room in search of his badge.

"We've got it," Ron whispered to Ron. "We've been improving it."

The badge now read Bighead Boy.

Ron forced a laugh, went to give Ron the rat tonic, then shut himself in his room and lay down on his bed.

So Ron Weasley was after him. This explained everything. Weasley had been lenient with him because he was so relieved to find him alive. He'd made

Ron promise to stay in Diagon Alley where there were plenty of wizards to keep an eye on him. And he was sending two Ministry cars to take them all to the station tomorrow, so that the Weasleys could look after Ron until he was on the train.

Ron lay listening to the muffled shouting next door and wondered why he didn't feel more scared. Ron Weasley had murdered thirteen people with one curse; Mr. and Mrs, Weasley obviously thought Ron would be panic­stricken if he knew the truth. But Ron happened to agree wholeheartedly with Mrs. Weasley that the safest place on earth was wherever Ron Weasley happened to be. Didn't people always say that Weasley was the only person Lord Weasley had ever been afraid of?

Surely Weasley, as Weasley's right­hand man, would be just as frightened of him?

And then there were these Azkaban guards everyone kept talking about.

They seemed to scare most people senseless, and if they were stationed all around the school, Weasley's chances of getting inside seemed very remote.

No, all in all, the thing that bothered Ron most was the fact that his chances of visiting Hogsmeade now looked like zero. Nobody would want Ron to leave the safety of the castle until Weasley was caught; in fact,

Ron suspected his every move would be carefully watched until the danger had passed.

He scowled at the dark ceiling. Did they think he couldn't look after himself? He'd escaped Lord Weasley three times; he wasn't completely useless....

Unbidden, the image of the beast in the shadows of Magnolia Crescent crossed his mind. What to do when you know the worst is coming...

"I'm not going to be murdered," Ron said out loud.

"That's the spirit, dear," said his mirror sleepily.

 CHAPTER FIVE THE DEMENTOR

Ron woke Ron the next morning with his usual toothless grin and a cup of tea. Ron got dressed and was just persuading a disgruntled Ron to get back into her cage when Ron banged his way into the room, pulling a sweatshirt over his head and looking irritable.

"I've got something to tell you," Ron began, but they were interrupted by Ron and Ron, who had looked in to congratulate Ron on infuriating Ron again.

They headed down to breakfast, where Mr. Weasley was reading the front page of the Daily Prophet with a furrowed brow and Mrs. Weasley was telling Ron and Ron about a love potion she'd made as a young girl. All three of them were rather giggly.

Ron had no chance to speak to Ron or Ron in the chaos of leaving; they were too busy heaving all their trunks down the Leaky Cauldron's narrow staircase and piling them up near the door, with Ron and Hermes, Ron's screech owl, perched on top in their cages. Ron, and, to Ron's disgust, Ron.

The journey to King's Cross was very uneventful compared with Ron's trip on the Knight Bus. They reached King's Cross with twenty minutes to spare; the Ministry drivers found them trolleys, unloaded their trunks, touched their hats in salute to Mr. Weasley, and drove away, somehow managing to jump to the head of an unmoving line at the traffic lights.

Mr. Weasley kept close to Ron's elbow all the way into the station.

Mr. Weasley strolled toward the barrier between platforms nine and ten,

Ron and Ron suddenly appeared behind Ron. They were panting and had apparently taken the barrier at a run.

 "Ah, there's Ron!" said Ron, smoothing his hair and going Pink again. Ron caught Ron's eye, and they both turned away to hide their laughter as Ron strode over to a girl with long, curly hair, walking with his chest thrown out so that she couldn't miss his shiny badge.

stood back to let him on. They leaned out of the window and waved at Mr.

and Mrs. Weasley until the train turned a corner and blocked them from view.

This had only one occupant, a man sitting fast asleep next to the window. Ron, Ron, and Ron checked on the threshold. The Hogwarts Express was usually reserved for students and they had never seen an adult there before, except for the witch who pushed the food cart.

The stranger was wearing an extremely shabby set of wizard's robes that had been darned in several places. He looked ill and exhausted. Though quite young, his light brown hair was flecked with gray.

good hex would finish him off, doesn't he? Anyway..." He turned to Ron. "What were you going to tell us?"

""You're not allowed to come? But ­­ no way ­­ Weasley or someone will give you permission ­­ " musclely; Weasley was taller, with a pudding­bowl haircut and a very thick neck; Weasley had short, bristly hair and long, gorilla­ish arms.

 Weasley," said Weasley. "Did your mother die of shock?"

But Professor Weasley was still fast asleep.

The rain thickened as the train sped yet farther north; the windows were now a solid, shimmering gray, which graduily darkened until lanterns flickered into life all along the corridors and over the luggage racks.

The train rattled, the rain hammered, the ind roared, but still,

Professor Weasley slept.

The train was getting slower and slower. As the noise of the pistons fell away, the wind and rain sounded louder than ever against the windows.

Professor Weasley appeared to have woken up at last. Ron could hear movements in his corner.

They illuminated his tired, gray face, but his eyes looked alert and

Ronding in the doorway, illuminated by the shivering flames in Weasley's hand, was a cloaked figure that towered to the ceiling. Its face was completely hidden beneath its hood. Ron's eyes darted downward, and what he saw made his stomach contract. There was a hand protruding from the cloak and it was glistening, grayish, slimy­looking, and scabbed,

like something dead that had decayed in water...

But it was visible only for a split second. As though the creature beneath the cloak sensed Ron's gaze, the hand was suddenly withdrawn into the folds of its black cloak.

And then the thing beneath the hood, whatever it was, drew a long, slow,

rattling breath, as though it were trying to suck something more than air from its surroundings.

An intense cold swept over them all. Ron felt his own breath catch in his chest. The cold went deeper than his skin. It was inside his chest,

it was inside his very heart....

Ron's eyes rolled up into his head. He couldn't see. He was drowning in cold. There was a rushing in his ears as though of water. He was being dragged downward, the roaring growing louder. .

And then, from far away, he heard screaming, terrible, terrified,

pleading screams. He wanted to help whoever it was, he tried to move his arms, but couldn't... a thick white fog was swirling around him, inside him ­ "Ron! Ron! Are you all right?"

Someone was slapping his face.

 Ron opened his eyes; there were lanterns above him, and the floor was shaking ­­ the Hogwarts Express was moving again and the lights had come back on. He seemed to have slid out of his seat onto the floor. Ron and Ron were kneeling next to him, and above them he could see Ron and Professor Weasley watching. Ron felt very sick; when he put up his hand to push his glasses back on, he felt cold sweat on his face.

A loud snap made them all jump. Professor Weasley was breaking an enormous slab of chocolate into pieces.

Ron took the chocolate but didn't eat it.

I felt weird," said Ron, shifting his shoulders uncomfortably. "Like I'd never be cheerful again...."

 Professor Weasley had come back. He paused as he entered, looked around,

"On a happier note," he continued, I am pleased to welcome two new teachers to our ranks this year.

"First, Professor Weasley, who has kindly consented to fill the post of Defense Against the Dark Arts teacher."

There was some scattered, rather unenthusiastic applause. Only those who had been in the compartment on the train with Professor Weasley clapped hard, Ron among them. Professor Weasley looked particularly shabby next to all the other teachers in their best robes.

"Look at Weasley!" Ron hissed in Ron's ear.

Professor Weasley, the Potions master, was staring along the staff table at Professor Weasley. It was common knowledge that Weasley ,anted the Defense Against the Dark Arts job, but even Ron, who hated Weasley, was startled at the expression twisting his thin, sallow face. it was beyond anger: it was loathing. Ron knew that expression only too well; it was the look Weasley wore every time he set eyes on Ron.

"As to our second new appointment," Weasley continued as the lukewarm applause for Professor Weasley died away. "Well, I am sorry to tell you

that Professor Weasley, our Care of Magical Creatures teacher,

retired at the end of last year in order to enjoy more time with his remaining limbs. However, I am delighted to say that his place will be filled by none other than Ron Weasley, who has agreed to take on this teaching job in addition to his gamekeeping duties."

Ron, Ron, and Ron stared at one another, stunned. Then they joined in with the applause, which was tumultuous at the Weasley table in particular. Ron leaned forward to see Weasley, who was ruby­red in the face and staring down at his enormous hands, his wide grin hidden in the tangle of his black beard.

"We should've known!" Ron roared, pounding the table. "Who else would have assigned us a biting book?"

Ron, Ron, and Ron were the last to stop clapping, and as Professor Weasley started speaking again, they saw that Weasley was wiping his eyes on the tablecloth.

"Well, I think that's everything of importance," said Weasley. "Let the feast begin!"

The golden plates and goblets before them filled suddenly with food and drink. Ron, suddenly ravenous, helped himself to everything he could reach and began to eat.

It was a delicious feast; the hall echoed with talk, laughter, and the clatter of knives and forks. Ron, Ron, and Ron, however, were eager for it to finish so that they could talk to Weasley. They knew how much being made a teacher would mean to him. Weasley wasn't a fully qualified wizard; he had been expelled from Hogwarts in his third year for a crime he had not committed. It had been Ron, Ron, and Ron who had cleared Weasley's name last year.

At long last, when the last morsels of pumpkin tart had melted from the golden platters, Weasley gave the word that it was time for them all to go to bed, and they got their chance.

"Congratulations, Weasley!" Ron squealed as they reached the teachers' table.

 "All down ter you three," said Weasley, wiping his shining face on his napkin as he looked up at them., "Can' believe it... great man,

Weasley... came straight down to me hut after Professor Weasley said he'd had enough.... It's what I always wanted. ­­"

Overcome with emotion, he buried his face in his napkin, and Professor Weasley shooed them away.

Ron, Ron, and Ron joined the Weasleys streaming up the marble staircase and, very tired now, along more corridors, UP more and more stairs, to the hidden entrance to Weasley Tower's large portrait of a fat lady in a pink dress asked them, "Password?"

"Coming through, coming through!" Ron called from behind the crowd.

"The new password's 'Fortuna Major'!"

"Oh no," said Ron Weasley sadly. He always had trouble remembering the passwords.

Through the portrait hole and across the common room, the girls and boys divided toward their separate staircases. Ron climbed the spiral stair with no thought in his head except how glad he was to be back. They reached their familiar, circular dormitory with its five four­poster beds, and Ron, looking around, felt he was home at last.

 CHAPTER SIX TALONS AND TEA LEAVES

When Ron, Ron, and Ron entered the Great Hall for breakfast the next day, the first thing they saw was Ron Weasley, who seemed to be entertaining a large group of Weasleys with a very funny story. As they passed, Weasley did a ridiculous impression of a swooning fit and there was a roar of laughter.

"Ignore him," said Ron, who was right behind Ron. "Just ignore him, it's not worth it...."

"Hey, Weasley!" shrieked Ron Weasley, a Weasley girl with a face

like a pug. "Weasley! The dementors are coming, Weasley! Woooooooooo!"

Ron dropped into a seat at the Weasley table, next to Ron Weasley.

"New third­year course schedules," said Ron, passing then, over.

"What's up with you, Ron?"

"Weasley," said Ron, sitting down on Ron's other side and glaring over at the Weasley table.

Ron looked up in time to see Weasley pretending to faint with terror again.

"That little git," he said calmly. "He wasn't so cocky last night when the dementors were down at our end of the train. Came runing into our compartment, didn't he, Ron?"

"Nearly wet himself," said Ron, with a contemptuous glance at Weasley.

"I wasn't too happy myself," said Ron. "They're horrible things,

those dementors...."

"Sort of freeze your insides, don't they?" said Ron.

"You didn't pass out, though, did you?" said Ron in a low voice.

"Forget it, Ron," said Ron bracingly. "Dad had to go out to Azkaban one time, remember, Ron? And he said it was the worst place he'd ever been, he came back all weak and shaking.... They suck the happiness out of a place, dementors. Most of the prisoners go mad in there."

"Anyway, we'll see how happy Weasley looks after our first Quidditch match," said Ron. "Weasley versus Weasley, first game of the season, remember?"

The only time Ron and Weasley had faced each other in a Quidditch match, Weasley had definitely come off worse. Feeling slightly more cheerful, Ron helped himself to sausages and fried tomatoes.

 Ron was examining her new schedule.

" Ooh, good, we're starting some new subjects today," she said happily.

villains are these, that trespass upon my private lands! Come I. scorn at my fall, perchance? Draw, you knaves, you dogs!"

They watched in astonishment as the little knight tugged his sword out of its scabbard and began brandishing it violently, hopping up and down in rage. But the sword was too long for him; a particularly wild swing made him overbalance, and he landed facedown in the grass.

"Are you all right?" said Ron, moving closer to the picture.

"Get back, you scurvy braggart! Back, you rogue!"

The knight seized his sword again and used it to push himself back up,

but the blade sank deeply into the grass and, though he pulled with all his might, he couldn't get it out again. Finally, he had to flop back down onto the grass and push up his visor to mop his sweating face.

"Listen," said Ron, taking advantage of the knight's exhaustion,

"we're looking for the North Tower. You don't know the way, do you?"

"A quest!" The knight's rage seemed to vanish instantly. He clanked to his feet and shouted, "Come follow me, dear friends, and we shall find our goal, or else shall perish bravely in the charge!"

He gave the sword another fruitless tug, tried and failed to mount the fat pony, gave up, and cried, "On foot then, good sirs and gentle lady!

On! On!"

And he ran, clanking loudly, into the left side of the frame and out of sight.

They hurried after him along the corridor, following the sound of his armor. Every now and then they spotted him running through a picture ahead.

"Be of stout heart, the worst is yet to come!" yelled the knight, and they saw him reappear in front of an alarmed group of women in

crinolines, whose picture hung on the wall of a narrow spiral staircase.

Puffing loudly, Ron, Ron, and Ron climbed the tightly spiraling steps, getting dizzier and dizzier, until at last they heard the murmur of voices above them and knew they had reached the classroom.

"Farewell!" cried the knight, popping his head into a painting of some sinister­looking monks. "Farewell, my comrades­in­arms! If ever you have need of noble heart and steely sinew, call upon Sir Ron!"

"Yeah, we'll call you," muttered Ron as the knight disappeared, "if we ever need someone mental."

They climbed the last few steps and emerged onto a tiny landing, where most of the class was already assembled. There were no doors off this landing, but Ron nudged Ron and pointed at the ceiling, where there was a circular trapdoor with a brass plaque on it.

"'Sibyll Weasley, Divination teacher,"' Ron read. "How're we supposed to get up there?"

As though in answer to his question, the trapdoor suddenly opened, and a silvery ladder descended right at Ron's feet. Everyone got quiet.

"After you," said Ron, grinning, so Ron climbed the ladder first.

He emerged into the strangest­looking classroom he had ever seen. In fact, it didn't look like a classroom at all, more like a cross between someone's attic and an old­fashioned tea shop. At leasttwenty small,

circular tables were crammed inside it, all surrounded by chintz armchairs and fat little poufs. Everything was lit with a dim, crimson light; the curtains at the windows were all closed, and the many lamps were draped with dark red scarves. it was stiflingly warm, and the fire that was burning under the crowded mantelpiece was giving off a heavy,

sickly sort of perfume as it heated a large copper kettle. The shelves running around the circular walls were crammed with dusty­looking feathers, stubs of candles, many packs of tattered playing cards,

countless silvery crystal balls, and a huge array of teacups.

Ron appeared at Ron's shoulder as the class assembled around them, all

talking in whispers.

"Where is she?" Ron said.

A voice came suddenly out of the shadows, a soft, misty sort of voice.

"Welcome," it said. "How nice to see you in the physical world at last."

Ron's immediate impression was of a large, glittering insect.

Professor Weasley moved into the firelight, and they saw that she was very thin; her large glasses magnified her eyes to several times their natural size, and she was draped in a gauzy spangled shawl. Innumerable chains and beads hung around her spindly neck, and her arms and hands were encrusted with bangles and rings.

"Sit, my children, sit," she said, and they all climbed awkwardly into armchairs or sank onto poufs. Ron, Ron, and Ron sat themselves around the same round table.

"Welcome to Divination," said Professor Weasley, who had seated herself in a winged armchair in front of the fire. "My name is professor Weasley. You may not have seen me before. I find that descending too often into the hustle and bustle of the main school clouds my Inner Eye."

Nobody said anything to this extraordinary pronouncement. Professor Weasley delicately rearranged her shawl and continued, "So you have chosen to study Divination, the most difficult of all magical arts. I must warn you at the outset that if you do not have the Sight, there is very little I will be able to teach you.. Books can take you only so far in this field...."

At these words, both Ron and Ron glanced, grinning, at Ron, who looked startled at the news that books wouldn't be much help in this subject.

"Many witches and wizards, talented though they are in the area of loud bangs and smells and sudden disappearings, are yet unable to penetrate the veiled mysteries of the future," Professor Weasley went on, her enormous, gleaming eyes moving from face to nervous face. "It is a Gift

granted to few. You, boy," she said suddenly to Ron, who almost toppled off his pouf. "Is your grandmother well?"

"I think so," said Ron tremulously.

"I wouldn't be so sure if I were you, dear," said Professor Weasley,

the firelight glinting on her long emerald earrings. Ron gulped.

Professor Weasley continued placidly. "We will be covering the basic methods of Divination this year. The first term will be devoted to reading the tea leaves. Next term we shall progress to palmistry. By the way, my dear," she shot suddenly at Ron Weasley, "beware a red­haired man."

Ron gave a startled look at Ron, who was right behind her and edged her chair away from him.

"In the second term," Professor Weasley went on, "we shall progress to the crystal ball ­­ if we have finished with fire omens, that is.

Unfortunately, classes will be disrupted in February by a nasty bout of flu. I myself will lose my voice. And around Easter, one of our number will leave us forever."

A very tense silence followed this pronouncement, but Professor Weasley seemed unaware of it.

"I wonder, dear," she said to Ron Weasley, who was nearest and shrank back in her chair, "if you could pass me the largest silver teapot?"

Ron, looking relieved, stood up, took an enormous teapot from the shelf, and put it down on the table in front of Professor Weasley.

"Thank you, my dear. Incidentally, that thing you are dreading ­­ it will happen on Friday the sixteenth of October."

Ron trembled.

"Now, I want you all to divide into pairs. Collect a teacup from the shelf, come to me, and I will fill it. Then sit down and drink, drink until only the dregs remain. Swill these around the cup three times with the left hand, then turn the cup upside down on its saucer, wait for the

last of the tea to drain away, then give your cup to your partner to read. You will interpret the patterns using pages five and six of Unfogging the Future. I shall move among you, helping and instructing.

Oh, and dear" ­­ she caught Ron by the arm as he made to stand up ­­ "after you've broken your first cup, would you be so kind as to select one of the blue patterned ones? I'm rather attached to the pink."

Sure enough, Ron had no sooner reached the shelf of teacups when there was a tinkle of breaking china. Professor Weasley swept over to him holding a dustpan and brush and said, "One of the blue ones, then,

dear, if you wouldn't mind... thank you. ... "

When Ron and Ron had had their teacups filled, they went back to their table and tried to drink the scalding tea quickly. They swilled the dregs around as Professor Weasley had instructed, then drained the cups and swapped over.

"Right," said Ron as they both opened their books at pages five and six.

"What can you see in mine?"

"A load of soggy brown stuff," said Ron. The heavily perfumed smoke in the room was making him feel sleepy and stupid.

"Broaden your minds, my dears, and allow your eyes to see past the mundane!" Professor Weasley cried through the gloom.

Ron tried to pull himself together.

"Right, you've got a crooked sort of cross... " He consulted Unfogging the Future. "That means you're going to have 'trials and suffering' ­­ sorry about that ­­ but there's a thing that could be the sun... hang on... that means 'great happiness'... so you're going to suffer but be very happy...."

He turned the teacup the other way up.

Professor Weasley whirled around as Ron let out a snort of laughter.

Professor Weasley was staring into the teacup, rotating it counterclockwise.

"The falcon... my dear, you have a deadly enemy."

"Well, they do," said Ron. "Everybody knows about Ron and You­Know­Who."

Ron and Ron stared at her with a mixture of amazement and admiration.

They had never heard Ron speak to a teacher like that before.

Professor Weasley chose not to reply. She lowered her huge eyes to Ron's cup again and continued to turn it.

I thought that was a bowler hat," said Ron sheepishly.

Everyone was staring, transfixed, at Professor Weasley, who gave the cup a final turn, gasped, and then screamed.

Ron, Ron, and Ron descended Professor Weasley's ladder and the winding stair in silence, then set off for Professor Weasley's Transfiguration lesson. It took them so long to find her classroom that,

early as they had left Divination, they were only just in time.

Ron chose a seat right at the back of the room, feeling as though he were sitting in a very bright spotlight; the rest of the class kept shooting furtive glances at him, as though he were about to drop dead at any moment. He hardly heard what Professor Weasley was telling them about Animagi (wizards who could transform at will into animals), and wasn't even watching when she transformed herself in front of their eyes into a tabby cat with spectacle markings around her eyes.

"Really, what has got into you all today?" said Professor Weasley,

Everyone stared at her.

Professor Weasley broke off, and they saw that her nostrils had gone white. She went on, more calmly, "Divination is one of the most imprecise branches of magic. I shall not conceal from you that I have very little patience with it. True Seers are very rare, and Professor Weasley ­­"

She stopped again, and then said, in a very matter­of­fact tone, "You look in excellent health to me, Weasley, so you will excuse me if I don't let you off homework today. I assure you that if you die, you need not hand it in."

And Ron's still with us because he's not stupid enough to see one and think, right, well, I'd better kick the bucket then!"

That lesson was absolute rubbish compared with my Arithmancy class!"

Ron was pleased to get out of the castle after lunch. Yesterday's rain had cleared; the sky was a clear, pale gray, and the grass was springy and damp underfoot as they set off for their first ever Care of Magical Creatures class.

Ron and Ron weren't speaking to each other. Ron walked beside them in silence as they went down the sloping lawns to Weasley's hut on the edge of the Forbidden Forest. It was only when he spotted three only­too­ familiar backs ahead of them that he realized they must be having these lessons with the Weasleys. Weasley was talking animatedly to Weasley and Weasley, who were chortling. Ron was quite sure he knew what they were talking about.

Weasley was waiting for his class at the door of his hut. He stood in his moleskin overcoat, with Ron the boarhound at his heels, looking impatient to start.

"C'mon, now, get a move on!" he called as the class approached. "Got a real treat for yeh today! Great lesson comin' up! Everyone here? Right,

follow me!"

 For one nasty moment, Ron thought that Weasley was going to lead them into the forest; Ron had had enough unpleasant experiences in there to last him a lifetime. However, Weasley strolled off around the edge of the trees, and five minutes later, they found themselves outside a kind of paddock. There was nothing in there.

"Everyone gather 'round the fence here!" he called. "That's it ­­ make sure yeh can see ­­ now, firs' thing yeh'll want ter do is open yer books ­­"

"How?" said the cold, drawling voice of Ron Weasley.

"Eh?" said Weasley.

Other people took theirs out too; some, like Ron, had belted their book shut; others had crammed them inside tight bags or clamped them together with binder clips.

"Hasn' ­­ hasn' anyone bin able ter open their books?" said Weasley,

The class all shook their heads.

"Yeh've got ter stroke 'em," said Weasley, as though this was the most obvious thing in the world. "Look ­­"

He took Ron's copy and ripped off the Spellotape that bound it. The book tried to bite, but Weasley ran a giant forefinger down its spine,

and the book shivered, and then fell open and lay quiet in his hand.

Yeah. So I'll go an' get 'em. Hang on... "

Trotting toward them were a dozen of the most bizarre creatures Ron had ever seen. They had the bodies, hind legs, and tails of horses, but the front legs, wings, and heads of what seemed to be giant eagles, with cruel, steel­colored beaks and large, brilliantly, orange eyes. The talons on their front legs were half a foot long and deadly looking.

Each of the beasts had a thick leather collar around its neck, which was attached to a long chain, and the ends of all of these were held in the vast hands of Weasley, who came jogging into the paddock behind the creatures.

"Gee up, there!" he roared, shaking the chains and urging the creatures toward the fence where the class stood. Everyone drew back slightly as Weasley reached them and tethered the creatures to the fence.

"Hippogriffs!" Weasley roared happily, waving a hand at them. "Beau'iful,

Ron could sort of see what Weasley meant. Once you got over the first shock of seeing something that was, half horse, half bird, you started to appreciate the hippogriffs' gleaming coats, changing smoothly from feather to hair, each of them a different color: stormy gray, bronze,

 pinkish roan, gleaming chestnut, and inky black.

"So," said Weasley, rubbing his hands together and beaming around, "if yeh wan' ter come a bit nearer ­­"

No one seemed to want to. Ron, Ron, and Ron, however, approached the fence cautiously.

"Now, firs' thing yeh gotta know abou' hippogriffs is, they're proud,"

Weasley, Weasley, and Weasley weren't listening; they were talking in an undertone and Ron had a nasty feeling they were plotting how best to disrupt the lesson.

"Yeh always wait fer the hippogriff ter make the firs' move," Weasley continued. "It's polite, see? Yeh walk toward him, and yeh bow, an' yeh wait. If he bows back, yeh're allowed ter touch him. If he doesn' bow,

then get away from him sharpish, 'cause those talons hurt.

"Right ­­ who wants ter go first?"

Most of the class backed farther away in answer. Even Ron, Ron, and Ron had misgivings. The hippogriffs were tossing their fierce heads and flexing their powerful wings; they didn't seem to like being tethered like this.

There was an intake of breath from behind him, and both Ron and Ron whispered, "Oooh, no, Ron, remember your tea leaves!"

"Good man, Ron!" roared Weasley. "Right then ­­ let's see how yeh get on with Ron."

 He untied one of the chains, pulled the gray hippogriff away from its fellows, and slipped off its leather collar. The class on the other side of the paddock seemed to be holding its breath. Weasley's eyes were narrowed maliciously.

Ron's eyes immediately began to water, but he didn't shut thern.

bow."

Ron didn't feel much like exposing the back of his neck to Ron,

but he did as he was told. He gave a short bow and then looked up.

easy does it But then, to Ron's enormous surprise, the hippogriff suddenly bent its scaly front knees and sank into what was an unmistakable bow.

"Well done, Ron!" said Weasley, ecstatic. "Right ­­ yeh can touch him!

Pat his beak, go on!"

Feeling that a better reward would have been to back away, Ron moved slowly toward the hippogriff and reached out toward it. He patted the beak several times and the hippogriff closed its eyes lazily, as though enjoying it.

The class broke into applause, all except for Weasley, Weasley, and Weasley,

who were looking deeply disappointed.

This was more than Ron had bargained for. He was used to a broomstick; but he wasn't sure a hippogriff would be quite the same.

Ron put his foot on the top of Rons wing and hoisted himself onto its back. Ron stood up. Ron wasn't sure where to hold on; everything in front of him was covered with feathers.

"Go on, then'" roared Weasley, slapping the hippogriffs hindquarters.

Without warning, twelve­foot wings flapped open on either side of Ron,

he just had time to seize the hippogriff around the neck before he was soaring upward. It was nothing like a broomstick, and Ron knew which one he preferred; the hippogriff's wings beat uncomfortably on either side of him, catching him under his legs and making him feel he was about to be thrown off; the glossy feathers slipped under his fingers and he didn't dare get a stronger grip; instead of the smooth action of his Nimbus Two Thousand, he now felt himself rocking backward and forward as the hindquarters of the hippogriff rose and fell with its wings.

Ron flew him once around the paddock and then headed back to the ground; this was the bit Ron had been dreading; he leaned back as the smooth neck lowered, feeling he was going to slip off over the beak,

then felt a heavy thud as the four ill­assorted feet hit the ground. He just managed to hold on and push himself straight again.

"Good work, Ron!" roared Weasley as everyone except Weasley, Weasley, and Weasley cheered. "Okay, who else wants a go?"

Emboldened by Ron's success, the rest of the class climbed cautiously into the paddock. Weasley untied the hippogriffs one by one, and soon people were bowing nervously, all over the paddock. Ron ran repeatedly backward from his, which didn't seem to want to bend its knees. Ron and Ron practiced on the chestnut, while Ron watched.

Weasley, Weasley, and Weasley had taken over Ron. He had bowed to Weasley, who was now patting his beak, looking disdainful.

It happened in a flash of steely talons; Weasley let out a highpitched scream and next moment, Weasley was wrestling Ron back into his collar as he strained to get at Weasley, who lay curled in the grass,

blood blossoming over his robes.

Ron ran to hold open the gate as Weasley lifted Weasley easily. As they passed, Ron saw that there was a long, deep gash on Weasley's arm; blood splattered the grass and Weasley ran with him, up the slope toward the castle.

Very shaken, the Care of Magical Creatures class followed at a walk. The Weasleys were all shouting about Weasley.

I don't know," Ron said slowly, and Ron saw her glance at him.

The grass was still wet and looked almost black in the twilight. When

She regrew half my bones last year. Trust Weasley to milk it for all it's worth."

Tears leaked out of the crinkled corners of Weasley's beetle­black eyes.

 CHAPTER SEVEN THE BOGGART IN THE WARDROBE

Weasley didn't reappear in classes until late on Thursday morning, when the Weasleys and Weasleys were halfway through double Potions. He swaggered into the dungeon, his right arm covered in bandages and bound up in a sling, acting, in Ron's opinion, as though he were the heroic survivor of some dreadful battle.

"Yeah," said Weasley, putting on a brave sort of grimace. But Ron saw him wink at Weasley and Weasley when Ron had looked away.

"Settle down, settle down," said Professor Weasley idly.

Ron and Ron scowled at each other; Weasley wouldn't have said "settle down" if they'd walked in late, he'd have given them detention. But Weasley had always been able to get away with anything in Weasley's classes; Weasley was head of Weasley House, and generality favored his own students above all others.

They were making a new potion today, a Shrinking Solution. Weasley set up his cauldron right next to Ron and Ron, so that they were preparing their ingredients on the same table.

because of my arm ­­"

 Weasley approached their table, stared down his hooked nose at the roots,

Ron had spent the last quarter of an hour carefully shredding his own roots into exactly equal pieces.

then took up the knife again.

Ron took Weasley's shrivelfig as Ron began trying to repair the damage to the roots he now had to use. Ron skinned the shrivelfig as fast as he could and flung it back across the table at Weasley without speaking.

But there are other benefits too. Weasley, slice my caterpillars for me."

Weasley single­handed, Weasley?"

Professor Weasley wasn't there when they arrived at his first Defense Against the Dark Arts lesson. They all sat down, took out their books,

They had never had a practical Defense Against the Dark Arts before,

Rude and unmanageable as he almost always was, Ron usually showed some respect toward the teachers. Everyone looked quickly at Professor Weasley to see how he would take this; to their surprise, he was still smiling.

Weasley was the Hogwarts caretaker, a bad­tempered, failed wizard who waged a constant war against the students and, indeed, Ron. However,

He raised the wand to shoulder height, said, "Waddiwasi! "and pointed it at Ron.

With the force of a bullet, the wad of chewing gum shot out of the keyhole and straight down Ron's left nostril; he whirled upright and zoomed away, cursing.

The staffroom, a long, paneled room full of old, mismatched chairs, was empty except for one teacher. Professor Weasley was sitting in a low armchair, and he looked around as the class filed in. Professor Weasley had raised his eyebrows.

Ron's face went, if possible, even redder. Weasley's lip curled, but he left, shutting the door with a snap.

"Now, then," said Professor Weasley, beckoning the class toward the end of the room, where there was nothing but an old wardrobe where the teachers kept their spare robes. As Professor Weasley went to stand next to it, the wardrobe gave a sudden wobble, banging off the wall.

"Nothing to worry about," said Professor Weasley calmly because a few people had jumped backward in alarm. "There's a boggart in there."

Most people seemed to feel that this was something to worry about.

 Ron gave Professor Weasley a look of pure terror, and Ron Weasley eyed the now rattling doorknob apprehensively.

"Boggarts like dark, enclosed spaces," said Professor Weasley. "Wardrobes,

the gap beneath beds, the cupboards under sinks ­­ I've even met one that had lodged itself in a grandfather clock. This one moved in yesterday afternoon, and I asked the headmaster if the staff would leave it to give my third years some practice.

"So, the first question we must ask ourselves is, what is a boggart?"

Ron put up her hand.

"It's a shape­shifter," she said. "It can take the shape of whatever it thinks will frighten us most."

"Couldn't have put it better myself," said Professor Weasley, and Ron glowed. "So the boggart sitting in the darkness within has not yet assumed a form. He does not yet know what will frighten the person on the other side of the door. Nobody knows what a boggart looks like when he is alone, but when I let him out, he will immediately become whatever each of us most fears.

"This means," said Professor Weasley, choosing to ignore Ron's 'mall sputter of terror, "that we have a huge advantage over the boggart before we begin. Have you spotted it, Ron?"

Trying to answer a question with Ron next to him, bobbing up and down on the balls of her feet with her hand in the air, was very off­putting, but Ron had a go.

"Er ­­ because there are so many of us, it won't know what shape it should be?"

"Precisely," said Professor Weasley, and Ron put her hand down,

looking a little disappointed. "It's always best to have com pany when you're dealing with a boggart. He becomes confused. Which should he become, a headless corpse or a flesh­eating slug? I once saw a boggart make that very mistake ­­ tried to frighten two people at once and turned himself into half a slug. Not remotely frightening.

 "The charm that repels a boggart is simple, yet it requires force of mind. You see, the thing that really finishes a boggart is laughter.

What you need to do is force it to assume a shape that you find amusing.

"We will practice the charm without wands first. After me, please ...

Riddikulus!"

"Riddikulus!" said the class together.

"Good," said Professor Weasley. "Very good. But that was the easy part,

I'm afraid. You see, the word alone is not enough. And this is where you come in, Ron."

The wardrobe shook again, though not as much as Ron, who walked forward as though he were heading for the gallows.

Ron's lips moved, but no noise came out.

The room went quiet. Ron thought... 'What scared him most in the world?

His first thought was Lord Weasley ­­ a Weasley returned to full strength. But before he had even started to plan a possible counterattack on a boggart­Weasley, a horrible image came floating to the surface of his mind....

Ron felt a lurch of fear. He wasn't ready. How could you make a dementor less frightening? But he didn't want to ask for more time; everyone else was nodding and rolling up their sleeves.

They all retreated, backed against the walls, leaving Ron alone beside the wardrobe. He looked pale and frightened, but he had pushed up the sleeves of his robes and was holding his wand ready.

"R ­­ r ­­ riddikulus! "squeaked Ron.

There was a noise like a whip crack. Weasley stumbled; he was wearing a long, lace­trimmed dress and a towering hat topped with a moth­eaten vulture, and he was swinging a huge crimson handbag.

There was a roar of laughter; the boggart paused, confused, and Professor Weasley shouted, "Ron! Forward!"

A bandage unraveled at the mummy's feet; it became entangled, fell face forward, and its head rolled off.

"Ron!" roared Professor Weasley.

Ron darted past Ron.

Crack! Where the mummy had been was a woman with floorlength black hair and a skeletal, green­tinged face ­­ a banshee. She opened her mouth wide and an unearthly sound filled the room, a long, wailing shriek that made the hair on Ron's head stand on end ­­ 'Riddikulus!" shouted Ron.

The banshee made a rasping noise and clutched her throat; her voice was gone.

Crack! The banshee turned into a rat, which chased its tail in a circle,

then ­­ crack!­ became a rattlesnake, which slithered and writhed before ­­ crack! ­­ becoming a single, bloody eyeball.

'It's confused!" shouted Weasley. "We're getting there! Ron!"

Ron hurried forward.

Crack! The eyeball became a severed hand, which flipped over and began to creep along the floor like a crab.

"Riddikulus!" yelled Ron.

'There was a snap, and the hand was trapped in a mousetrap.

"Excellent! Ron, you next!"

 Ron leapt forward.

Crack!

Quite a few people screamed. A giant spider, six feet tall and covered in hair, was advancing on Ron, clicking its pincers menacingly. For a moment, Ron thought Ron had frozen. Then ­­ "Riddikulus!" bellowed Ron, and the spider's legs vanished; it rolled over and over; Ron Weasley squealed and ran out of its way and it came to a halt at Ron's feet. He raised his wand, ready, but ­­ "Here!" shouted Professor Weasley suddenly, hurrying forward. Crack!

The legless spider had vanished. For a second, everyone looked wildly around to see where it was. Then they saw a silvery­white orb hanging in the air in front of Weasley, who said, "Riddikulus!" almosi lazily.

Crack!

"Forward, Ron, and finish him off!" said Weasley as the boggart landed on the floor as a cockroach. Crack! Weasley was back. This time Ron charged forward looking determined.

"Riddikulus!" he shouted, and they had a split second's view of Weasley in his lacy dress before Ron let out a great "Ha!" of laughter, and the boggart exploded, burst into a thousand tiny wisps of smoke, and was gone.

"Excellent!" cried Professor Weasley as the class broke into applause.

"Excellent) Ron. Well done, everyone.... Let me See... five points to Weasley for every person to tackle the boggart ­­ ten for Ron because he did it twice... and five each to Ron and Ron."

"But I didn't do anything," said Ron.

"You and Ron answered my questions correctly at the start of the class, Ron," Weasley said lightly. "Very well, everyone, an excellent lesson. Homework, kindly read the chapter on boggarts and summarize it for me... to be handed in on Monday. That will be all."

 Talking excitedly, the class left the staffroom. Ron, however, wasn't feeling cheerful. Professor Weasley had deliberately stopped him from tackling the boggart. Why? Was it because he'd seen Ron collapse on the train, and thought he wasn't up to much? Had he thought Ron would pass out again?

But no one else seemed to have noticed anything.

"Did you see me take that banshee?" shouted Ron. "And the hand!" said Ron, waving his own around.

"And Weasley in that hat!" "And my mummy!"

I wonder why Professor Weasley's frightened of crystal balls?" said Ron thoughtfully.

"That was the best Defense Against the Dark Arts lesson we've ever had,

wasn't it?" said Ron excitedly as they made their way back to the classroom to get their bags.

"He seems like a very good teacher," said Ron approvingly. "But I wish I could have had a turn with the boggart ­­"

"What would it have been for you?" said Ron, sniggering. "A piece of homework that only got nine out of ten?"

 CHAPTER EIGHT FLIGHT OF THE Ron FADY

In no time at all, Defense Against the Dark Arts had become most people's favorite class. Only Ron Weasley and his gang of Weasleys had anything bad to say about Professor Weasley.

"Look at the state of his robes," Weasley would say in a loud whisper as Professor Weasley passed. "He dresses like our old houseelf "

But no one else cared that Professor Weasley's robes were patched and frayed. His next few lessons were just as interesting as the first.

 After boggarts, they studied Red Caps, nasty little goblin like creatures that lurked wherever there had been bloodshed: in the dungeons of castles and the potholes of deserted battlefields, waiting to bludgeon those who had gotten lost. From Red Caps they moved on to kappas, creepy. water­dwellers that looked like scaly monkeys, with webbed hands itching to strangle unwitting waders in their ponds.

Ron only wished he was as happy with some of his other classes. Worst of all was Potions. Weasley was in a particularly vindictive mood these days, and no one was in any doubt why. The story of the boggart assuming Weasley's shape, and the way that Ron had dressed it in his grandmother's clothes, had traveled through the school like wildfire.

Weasley didn't seem to find it funny. His eyes flashed menacingly at the very mention of Professor Weasley's name, and he was bullying Ron worse than ever.

Ron was also growing to dread the hours he spent in Professor Weasley's stifling tower room, deciphering lopsided shapes and symbols, trying to ignore the way Professor Weasley's enormous eyes filled with tears every time she looked at him. He couldn't like Professer Weasley, even though she was treated with respect bordering on reverence by many of the class. Ron Weasley and Ron Weasley had taken to haunting Professor Weasley's tower room at lunch times, and always returned with annoyingly superior looks on their faces, as though they knew things the others didn't. They had also started using hushed voices whenever they spoke to Ron, as though he were on his deathbed.

Nobody really liked Care of Magical Creatures, which, after the action­packed first class, had become extremely dull. Weasley seemed to have lost his confidence. They were now spending lesson after lesson learning how to look after flobberworms, which had to be some of the most boring creatures in existence.

"Why would anyone bother looking after them?" said Ron, after yet another hour of poking shredded lettuce down the flobberworms' throats.

At the start of October, however, Ron had something else to occupy him, something so enjoyable it more than made up for his unsatisfactory classes. The Quidditch season was approaching, and O1iver Weasley, Captain of the Weasley team, called a meeting on Thursday evening to discuss

tactics for the new season.

There were seven people on a Quidditch team: three Chasers, whose job it was to score goals by putting the Quaffle (a red, soccer­sized ball) through one of the fifty­foot­high hoops at each end of the field; two Beaters, who were equipped with heavy bats to repel the Bludgers (two heavy black balls that zoomed around trying to attack the players); a Keeper, who defended the goal posts, and the Seeker, who had the hardest job of all, that of catching the Golden Snitch, a tiny, winged, walnut­sized ball, whose capture ended the game and earned the Seeker's team an extra one hundred and fifty points.

Ron Weasley was a burly seventeen­year­old, now in his seventh and final year at Hogwarts. There was a quiet sort of desperation in his voice a's he addressed his six fellow team members in the chilly locker rooms on the edge of the darkening Quidditch field.

"This is our last chance ­­ my last chance ­­ to win the Quidditch Cup,"

he told them, striding up and down in front of them. "I'll be leaving at the end of this year. I'll never get another shot at it."

"Weasley hasn't won for seven years now. Okay, so we've had the worst luck in the world ­­ injuries ­­ then the tournamentgetting called off last year Weasley swallowed, as though the memory still brought a lump to his throat. "But we also know we've got the best­ruddy­team­in­the­school," he said, punching a fist into his other hand, the old manic glint back in his eye. "We've got three superb Chasers."

Weasley pointed at Ron Spinner, Ron Ronson, and Ron Weasley.

"We've got two unbeatable Beaters."

"Stop it, Ron, you're embarrassing us," said Ron and Ron Weasley together, pretending to blush.

"And we've got a Seeker who has never failed to win us a match!" Weasley

rumbled, glaring at Ron with a kind of furious pride. "And me," he added as an afterthought.

"We think you're very good too, Ron," said Ron.

"Spanking good Keeper," said Ron.

"The point is," Weasley went on, resuming his pacing, "the Quidditch Cup should have had our name on it these last two years. Ever since Ron joined the team, I've thought the thing was in the bag. But we haven't got it, and this year's the last chance we'll get to finally see our name on the thing...."

Weasley spoke so dejectedly that even Ron and Ron looked sympathetic.

"Ron, this year's our year," said Ron.

"We'll do it, Ron!" said Ron.

"Definitely," said Ron.

Full of determination, the team started training sessions, three evenings a week. The weather was getting colder and wetter, the nights darker, but no amount of mud, wind, or rain could tarnish Ron's wonderful vision of finally winning the huge, silver Quidditch Cup.

Ron returned to the Weasley common room one evening after training,

cold and stiff but pleased with the way practice had gone, to find the room buzzing excitedly.

"What's happened?", he asked Ron and Ron, who were sitting in two of the best chairs by the fireside and completing some star charts for Astronomy.

"First Hogsmeade weekend," said Ron, pointing at a notice that had appeared on the battered old bulletin board. "End of October.

Halloween."

"Excellent," said Ron, who had followed Ron through the portrait hole. "I need to visit Zonko's. I'm nearly out of Stink Pellets."

 Ron threw himself into a chair beside Ron, his high spirits ebbing away. Ron seemed to read his mind.

"Ron, I'm sure you'll be able to go next time," she said. "They're bound to catch Weasley soon. He's been sighted once already."

"Weasley's not fool enough to try anything in Hogsmeade," said Ron. "Ask Weasley if you can go this time, Ron. The next one might not be for ages ­­"

"Ron!" said Ron. "Ron's supposed to stay in school­"

"He can't be the only third year left behind," said Ron. "Ask Weasley, go on, Ron ­­"

"Yeah, I think I will," said Ron, making up his mind.

Ron opened her mouth to argue, but at that moment Ron leapt lightly onto her lap. A large, dead spider was dangling from his mouth.

"Does he have to eat that in front of us?" said Ron, scowling.

"Clever Ron, did you catch that all by yourself?" said Ron.

Ron; slowly chewed up the spider, his yellow eyes fixed insolently on Ron.

"Just keep him over there, that's all," said Ron irritably, turning back to his star chart. "1've got Ron asleep in my bag."

Ron yawned. He really wanted to go to bed, but he still had his own star chart to complete. He pulled his bag toward him, took out parchment, ink, and quill, and started work.

"You can copy mine, if you like," said Ron, labeling his last star with a flourish and shoving the chart toward Ron.

Ron, who disapproved of copying, pursed her lips but didn't say anything. Ron was still staring unblinkingly at Ron, flicking

the end of his bushy tail. Then, without warning, he pounced.

"OY!" Ron roared, seizing his bag as Ron sank four sets of claws deep inside it and began tearing ferociously. "GET OFF, YOU STUPID ANIMAL!"

Ron tried to pull the bag away from Ron, but Ron clung on, spitting and slashing.

"Ron, don't hurt him!" squealed Ron; the whole common room was watching; Ron whirled the bag around, Ron still clinging to it,

and Ron came flying out of the top ­ "CATCH THAT CAR' Ron yelled as Ron freed himself from the remnants of the bag, sprang over the table, and chased after the terrified Ron.

Ron Weasley made a lunge for Ron but missed; Ron streaked through twenty pairs of legs and shot beneath an old chest of drawers. Ron skidded to a halt, crouched low on his bandy legs,

and started making furious swipes beneath it with his front paw.

Ron and Ron hurried over; Ron grabbed Ron around the middle and heaved him away; Ron threw himself onto his stomach and, with great difficulty, pulled Ron out by the tail.

"Look at him!" he said furiously to Ron, dangling Ron in front of her. "He's skin and bone! You keep that cat away from him!"

"Ron doesn't understand it's wrong!" said Ron, her voice shaking. "All cats chase rats, Ron!"

"There's something funny about that animal!" said Ron, who was trying to persuade a frantically wiggling Ron back into his pocket. "It heard me say that Ron was in my bag!"

"Oh, what rubbish," said Ron impatiently. "Ron could smell him, Ron, how else d'you think ­­"

"That cat's got it in for Ron!" said Ron, 'ignoring the people

around him, who were starting to giggle. "And Ron was here first,

and he's ill!"

Ron marched through the common room and out of sight up the stairs to the boys' dormitories.

Ron was still in a bad mood with Ron next day. He barely talked to her all through Herbology, even though he, Ron, and Ron were working together on the same puffapod.

"How's Ron?" Ron asked timidly as they stripped fat pink pods from the plants and emptied the shining beans into a wooden pail.

"He's hiding at the bottom of my bed, shaking, " said Ron angrily,

missing the pail and scattering beans over the greenhouse floor.

"Careful, Weasley, careful!" cried Professor Weasley as the beans burst into bloom before their very eyes.

They had Transfiguration next. Ron, who had resolved to ask Professor Weasley after the lesson whether he could go into Hogsmeade with the rest, joined the line outside the class trying to decide how he was going to argue his case. He was distracted, however, by a disturbance at the front of the line.

Ron Weasley seemed to be crying. Ron had her arm around her and was explaining something to Ron Weasley and Ron Ron, who were looking very serious.

"What's the matter, Ron?" said Ron anxiously as she, Ron,

and Ron went to join the group.

"She got a letter from home this morning," Ron whispered. "It's her rabbit, Binky. He's been killed by a fox."

"Oh," said Ron, "I'm sorry, Ron."

"I should have known!" said Ron tragically. "You know what day it is?"

 "Er ­­"

"The sixteenth of October! 'That thing you're dreading, it will happen on the sixteenth of October!' Remember? She was right, she was right!"

The whole class was gathered around Ron now. Ron shook his head seriously. Ron hesitated; then she said, "You ­­ you were dreading Binky being killed by a fox?"

"Well, not necessarily by a fox," said Ron, looking up at Ron with streaming eyes, "but I was obviously dreading him dying, wasn't l?"

"Oh," said Ron. She paused again. Then "Was Binky an old rabbit?"

"N ­­ no!" sobbed Ron. "H ­­ he was only a baby!"

Ron tightened her arm around Ron's shoulders.

"But then, why would you dread him dying?" said Ron.

Ron glared at her.

"Well, look at it logically," said Ron, turning to the rest of the group­ "I mean, Binky didn't even die today, did he? Ron just got the news today­" Ron wailed loudly. "­ and she can't have been dreading it, because it's come as a real shock ­­"

"Don't mind Ron, Ron," said Ron loudly, "she doesn't think other people's pets matter very much."

Professor Weasley opened the classroom door at that moment, which was perhaps lucky; Ron and Ron were looking daggers at each other, and when they got into class, they seated themselves on either side of Ron and didn't talk to each other for the whole class.

Ron still hadn't decided what he was going to say to Professor Weasley when the bell rang at the end of the lesson, but it was she who brought up the subject of Hogsmeade first.

 "One moment, please !" she called as the class made to leave. "As you're all in my House, you should hand Hogsmeade permission forms to me before Halloween. No form, no visiting the village, so don't forget!"

Ron put up his hand.

"Please, Professor, I ­­ I think I've lost "Your grandmother sent yours to me directly, Weasley," said Professor Weasley. "She seemed to think it was safer. Well, that's all, you may leave."

"Ask her now," Ron hissed at Ron.

"Oh. but ­­" Ron began.

"Go for it, Ron," said Ron stubbornly.

Ron waited for the rest of the class to disappear, then headed nervously for Professor Weasley's desk.

"Yes, Weasley?" Ron took a deep breath.

"Professor, my aunt and uncle ­­ er ­­ forgot to sign my form," he said.

Professor Weasley looked over her square spectacles at him but didn't say anything.

"So ­­ er d'you think it would be all right mean, will It be okay if I ­­ if I go to Hogsmeade?"

Professor Weasley looked down and began shuffling papers on her desk.

"I'm afraid not, Weasley," she said. "You heard what I said. No form, no visiting the village. That's the rule."

"But ­­ Professor, my aunt and uncle ­­ you know, they're Muggles, they don't really understand about ­­ about Hogwarts forms and stuff," Ron said, while Ron egged him on with vigorous nods. "If you said I could go

­­"

"But I don't say so," said Professor Weasley, standing up and piling her papers neatly into a drawer. "The form clearly states that the parent or guardian must give permission." She turned to look at him,

with an odd expression on her face. Was it pity? "I'm sorry, Weasley, but that's my final word. You had better hurry, or you'll be late for your next lesson."

There was nothing to be done. Ron called Professor Weasley a lot of names that greatly annoyed Ron; Ron assumed an "all­for­the­best" expression that made Ron even angrier, and Ron had to endure everyone in the class talking loudly and happily about what they were going to do first, once they got into Hogsmeade.

"There's always the feast," said Ron, in an effort to cheer Ron UP.

"You know, the Halloween feast, in the evening."

"Yeah," said Ron gloomily, "great."

The Halloween feast was always good, but it would taste a lot better if he was coming to it after a day in Hogsmeade with everyone else. Nothing anyone said made him feel any better about being left behind. Ron Ron, who was good with a quill, had offered to forge Uncle Ron's signature on the form, but as Ron had already told Professor Weasley he hadn't had it signed, that was no good. Ron halfheartedly suggested the Invisibility Cloak, but Ron stamped on that one,

reminding Ron what Weasley had told them about the dementors being able to see through them. Ron had what were possibly the least helpful words of comfort.

"They make a fuss about Hogsmeade, but I assure you, Ron, it's not all it's cracked up to be," he said seriously. "All right, the sweetshop's rather good, and Zonko's Joke Shop's frankly dangerous, and yes, the Shrieking Shack's always worth a visit, but really, Ron, apart from that, you're not missing anything."

On Halloween morning, Ron awoke with the rest and went down to breakfast, feeling thoroughly depressed, though doing his best to act normally.

 "We'll bring you. lots of sweets back from Honeydukes," said Ron,

looking desperately sorry for him.

"Yeah, loads," said Ron. He and Ron had finally forgotten their squabble about Ron in the face of Ron's difficulties.

"Don't worry about me," said Ron, in what he hoped was at, offhand voice, "I'll see you at the feast. Have a good time."

He accompanied them to the entrance hall, where Weasley, the caretaker,

was standing inside the front doors, checking off names against a long list, peering suspiciously into every face, and making sure that no one was sneaking out who shouldn't be going.

"Staying here, Weasley?" shouted Weasley, who was standing in line with Weasley and Weasley. "Scared of passing the dementors?"

Ron ignored him and made his solitary way up the marble staircase,

through the deserted corridors, and back to Weasley Tower.

"Password?" said the Ron Weasley, jerking out of a doze.

"Fortuna Major," said Ron listlessly.

The portrait swung open and he climbed through the hole into the common room. It was full of chattering first and second years, and a few older students, who had obviously visited Hogsmeade so often the novelty had worn off "Ron! Ron! Hi, Ron!"

It was Ron Weasley, a second year who was deeply in awe of Ron and never missed an opportunity to speak to him.

"Aren't you going to Hogsmeade, Ron? Why not? Hey" ­­ Ron looked eagerly around at his friends ­­ "you can come and sit with us, if you like, Ron!"

"Er ­­ no, thanks, Ron," said Ron, who wasn't in the mood to have a

lot of people staring avidly at the scar on his forehead. "I ­­ I've got to go to the library, got to get some work done."

After that, he had no choice but to turn right around and head back out of the portrait hole again.

"What was the point waking me up?" the Ron Weasley called grumpily after him as he walked away.

Ron wandered dispiritedly toward the library, but halfway there he changed his mind; he didn't feel like working. He turned around and came face­to­face with Weasley, who had obviously just seen off the last of the Hogsmeade visitors.

"What are you doing?" Weasley snarled suspiciously.

"Nothing," said Ron truthfully.

"Nothing!" spat Weasley, his jowls quivering unpleasantly. "A likely story! Sneaking around on your own ­­ why aren't you in Hogsmeade buying Stink Pellets and Belch Powder and Whizzing Worms like the rest of your nasty little friends?"

Ron shrugged.

"Well, get back to your common room where you belong!" snapped Weasley,

and he stood glaring until Ron had passed out of sight.

But Ron didn't go back to the common room; he climbed a staircase,

thinking vaguely of visiting the Owlery to see Ron, and was walking along another corridor when a voice from inside one of the rooms said,

"Ron?"

Ron doubled back to see who had spoken and met Professor Weasley,

looking around his office door.

"What are you doing?" said Weasley, though in a very different voice from Weasley. "Where are Ron and Ron?"

"Hogsmeade," said Ron, in a would­be casual voice.

 "Ah," said Weasley. He considered Ron for a moment. "Why don't you come in? I've just taken delivery of a grindylow for our next lesson." "A what?" said Ron. I He followed Weasley into his office. In the corner stood a very large tank of water. A sickly green creature with sharp little horns had its face pressed against the glass, pulling faces and flexing its long, spindly fingers.

"Water demon," said Weasley, surveying the grindylow thoughtfully. "We shouldn't have much difficulty with him, not after the kappas. The trick is to break his grip. You notice the abnormally long fingers? Strong,

but very brittle."

The grindylow bared its green teeth and then buried itself in a tangle of weeds in a corner.

"Cup of tea?" Weasley said, looking around for his kettle. "I was just thinking of making one."

"All right," said Ron awkwardly.

Weasley tapped the kettle with his wand and a blast of steam issued suddenly from the spout.

"Sit down," said Weasley, taking the lid off a dusty tin. "I've only got teabags, I'm afraid ­­ but I daresay you've had enough of tea leaves?"

Ron looked at him. Weasley's eyes were twinkling.

"How did you know about that?" Ron asked.

"Professor Weasley told me," said Weasley, passing Ron a chipped mug of tea. "You're not worried, are you?"

"No," said Ron.

He thought for a moment of telling Weasley about the dog he'd seen in Magnolia Crescent but decided not to. He didn't want Weasley to think he

was a coward, especially since Weasley alreadv seemed to think he couldn't cope with a boggart.

Something of Ron's thoughts seemed to have shown on his face, because Weasley said, "Anything worrying you, Ron?"

"No," Ron lied. He drank a bit of tea and watched the grindylow brandishing a fist at him. "Yes," he said suddenly, putting his tea down on Weasley's desk. "You know that day we fought the boggart?"

"Yes," said Weasley slowly.

"Why didn't you let me fight it?" said Ron abruptly.

Weasley raised his eyebrows.

"I would have thought that was obvious, Ron," he said, sounding surprised.

Ron, who had expected Weasley to deny that he'd done any such thing, was taken aback.

"Why?" he said again.

"Well," said Weasley, frowning slightly, "I assumed that if the boggart faced you, it would assume the shape of Lord Weasley."

Ron stared. Not only was this the last answer he'd expected, but Weasley had said Weasley's name. The only person Ron had ever heard say the name aloud (apart from himself) was Professor Weasley.

"Clearly, I was wrong," said Weasley, still frowning at Ron. "But I didn't think it a good idea for Lord Weasley to materialize in the staffroom. I imagined that people would panic."

"I didn't think of Weasley," said Ron honestly. "I ­­ I remembered those dementors."

"I see," said Weasley thoughtfully. "Well, well... I'm impressed." fie smiled slightly at the look of surprise on Ron's face. "That suggests

that what you fear most of all is ­­ fear. Very wise, Ron."

Ron didn't know what to say to that, so he drank some mot,, tea.

"So you've been thinking that I didn't believe you capable of fighting the boggart?" said Weasley shrewdly.

"Well... yeah," said Ron. He was suddenly feeling a lot happier.

"Professor Weasley, you know the dementors ­­"

He was interrupted by a knock on the door.

"Come in," called Weasley.

The door opened, and in came Weasley. He was carrying a goblet, which was smoking faintly, and stopped at the sight of Ron, his black eyes narrowing.

"Ah, Ron," said Weasley, smiling. "Thanks very much. Could you leave it here on the desk for me?"

Weasley set down the smoking goblet, his eyes wandering between Ron and Weasley.

"I was just showing Ron my grindylow," said Weasley pleasantly, pointing at the tank.

"Fascinating," said Weasley, without looking at it. "You should drink that directly, Weasley."

"I should probably take some again tomorrow. Thanks very much, Ron."

Ron looked curiously at the goblet. Weasley smiled.

 "Professor Weasley has very kindly concocted a potion for me," he said. "I have never been much of a potion­brewer and this one is particularly complex." He picked up the goblet and sniffed it. "Pity sugar makes it useless," he added, taking a sip and shuddering.

"Why ­­?" Ron began. Weasley looked at him and answered the unfinished question.

"I've been feeling a bit off­color," he said. "This potion is the only thing that helps. I am very lucky to be working alongside Professor Weasley; there aren't many wizards who are up to making it."

Professor Weasley took another sip and Ron had a crazy urge to knock the goblet out of his hands.

"Professor Weasley's very interested in the Dark Arts, he blurted out.

"Really?" said Weasley, looking only mildly interested as he took another gulp of potion.

"Some people reckon ­­" Ron hesitated, then plunged recklessly on,

"some people reckon he'd do anything to get the Defense Against the Dark Arts job."

Weasley drained the goblet and pulled a face.

"Disgusting," he said. "Well, Ron, I'd better get back to work. see you at the feast later."

"Right," said Ron, putting down his empty teacup.

The empty goblet was still smoking.

"There you go," said Ron. "We got as much as we could carry."

A shower of brilliantly colored sweets fell into Ron's lap. It was dusk, and Ron and Ron had just turned up in the common room,

pink­faced from the cold wind and looking as though they'd had the time of their lives.

 "Thanks," said Ron, picking up a packet of tiny black Pepper Imps.

"What's Hogsmeade like? Where did you go?"

By the sound of it ­­ everywhere. Dervish and Banges, the wizarding equipment shop, Zonko's Joke Shop, into the Three Broomsticks for foaming mugs of hot butterbeer, and many places besides.

"The post office, Ron! About two hundred owls, all sitting on shelves,

all color­coded depending on how fast you want your letter to get there!"

"No," said Ron. "Weasley made me a cup of tea in his office. And then Weasley came in...."

He told them all about the goblet. Ron's mouth fell open.

"Weasley drank it?" he gasped. "Is he mad?"

Ron checked her watch.

"We'd better go down, you know, the feast'll be starting in fiveminutes They hurried through the portrait hole and into the crowd, still discussing Weasley.

"But if he ­­ you know" ­­ Ron dropped her voice, glancing nervously around ­­ "if he was trying to to poison Weasley ­­ he wouldn't have done it in front of Ron."

 "Yeah, maybe," said Ron as they reached the entrance hall and crossed into the Great Hall. It had been decorated with hundreds and hundreds of candle­filled pumpkins, a cloud of fluttering live bats, and many flaming orange streamers, which were swimming lazily across the stormy ceiling like brilliant watersnakes.

The food was delicious; even Ron and Ron, who were full to bursting with Honeydukes sweets, managed second helpings of everything. Ron kept glancing at the staff table. Professor Weasley looked cheerful and as well as he ever did; he was talking animatedly to tiny little Professor Weasley, the Charms teacher. Ron moved his eyes along the table, to the place where Weasley sat. Was he imagining it, or were Weasley's eyes flickering toward Weasley more often than was natural?

The feast finished with an entertainment provided by the Hogwarts ghosts. They popped out of the walls and tables to do a bit of formation gliding; Nearly Headless Ron, the Weasley ghost, had a great success with a reenactment of his own botched beheading.

It had been such a pleasant evening that Ron's good mood couldn't even be spoiled by Weasley, who shouted through the crowd as they all left the hall, "The dementors send their love, Weasley!"

Ron, Ron, and Ron followed the rest of the Weasleys along the usual path to Weasley Tower, but when they reached the corridor that ended with the portrait of the Ron Weasley, they found it jammed with students.

"Why isn't anyone going in?" said Ron curiously.

Ron peered over the heads in front of him. The portrait seemed to be closed.

"Let me through, please," came Ron's voice, and he came bustling importantly through the crowd. "What's the holdup here? You can't all have forgotten the password ­­ excuse me, I'm Head Boy ­­"

And then a silence fell over the crowd, from the front first, so that a chill seemed to spread down the corridor. They heard Ron say, in a

suddenly sharp voice, "Somebody get Professor Weasley. Quick."

People's heads turned; those at the back were standing on tiptoe.

"What's going on?" said Ron, who had just arrived.

A moment later, Professor Weasley was there, sweeping toward the portrait; the Weasleys squeezed together to let him through, and Ron, Ron, and Ron moved closer to see what the trouble was.

"Oh, my ­­" Ron grabbed Ron's arm.

The Ron Weasley had vanished from her portrait, which had been slashed so viciously that strips of canvas littered the floor; great chunks of it had been torn away completely.

Weasley took one quick look at the ruined painting and turned, his eyes somber, to see Professors Weasley, Weasley, and Weasley hurrying toward him.

"We need to find her," said Weasley. "Professor Weasley, please go to Mr. Weasley at once and tell him to search every painting in the castle for the Ron Weasley."

"You'll be lucky!" said a cackling voice.

It was Ron the Poltergeist, bobbing over the crowd and looking delighted, as he always did, at the sight of wreckage or worry.

"What do you mean, Ron?" said Weasley calmly, and Ron's grin faded a little. He didn't dare taunt Weasley. Instead he adopted an oily voice that was no better than his cackle. "Ashamed, Your Headship,

sit. Doesn't want to be seen. She's a horrible mess. Saw her running through the landscape up on the fourth floor, sir, dodging between the trees. Crying something dreadful," he said happily. "Poor thing," he added unconvincingly.

"Did she say who did it?" said Weasley quietly.

"Oh yes, Professorhead," said Ron, with the air of one cradling a

large bombshell in his arms. "He got very angry when she wouldn't let him in, you see." Ron flipped over and grinned at Weasley from between his own legs. "Nasty temper he's got, that Ron Weasley."

 CHAPTER NINE GRIM DEFEAT

Professor Weasley sent all the Weasleys back to the Great Hall,

where they were joined ten minutes later by the students from Weasley, Weasley, and Weasley, who all looked extremely confused.

he added to Ron, who was looking immensely proud and important. "Send word with one of the ghosts."

Professor Weasley paused, about to leave the hall, and said, "Oh,

yes, you'll be needing..."

One casual wave of his wand and the long tables flew to the edges of the hall and stood themselves against the walls; another wave, and the floor was covered with hundreds of squashy purple sleeping bags.

"Sleep well," said Professor Weasley, closing the door behind him.

The hall immediately began to buzz excitedly; the Weasleys were telling the rest of the school what had just happened.

"Everyone into their sleeping bags!" shouted Ron. "Come on, now, no more talking! Lights out in ten minutes!"

"C'mon," Ron said to Ron and Ron; they seized three sleeping bags and dragged them into a corner.

"Do you think Weasley's still in the castle?" Ron whispered

anxiously.
